		<title>Build a semantic layer for agentic AI on AWS with Stardog and Amazon Bedrock AgentCore</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/build-a-semantic-layer-for-agentic-ai-on-aws-with-stardog-and-amazon-bedrock-agentcore/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Navin Sharma]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Fri, 10 Jul 2026 15:31:12 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Advanced (300)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Athena]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Bedrock]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Bedrock AgentCore]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon OpenSearch Service]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Redshift]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Simple Storage Service (S3)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[AWS Lake Formation]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Strands Agents]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Technical How-to]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">f38693695f066814841d089783bdd8f01dece5c1</guid>

					<description>In this post we show how to build a semantic layer on AWS using Stardog’s Semantic AI Application over Amazon Aurora and Amazon Redshift, and how to run a Strands Agents agent on Amazon Bedrock AgentCore that queries the layer to answer customer 360 questions across both sources without extract, transform, and load (ETL). The same Stardog deployment works behind AWS computes (Amazon Elastic Kubernetes Service (Amazon EKS), Amazon Elastic Container Service (Amazon ECS), and AWS Lambda). We use AgentCore here because it bundles inbound auth, hosting, and tool credentials into one managed service.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;In this post we show how to build a semantic layer on AWS using Stardog’s Semantic AI Application over Amazon Aurora and Amazon Redshift, and how to run a &lt;a href="https://github.com/strands-agents" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Strands Agents&lt;/a&gt; agent on Amazon Bedrock AgentCore that queries the layer to answer customer 360 questions across both sources without extract, transform, and load (ETL). The same Stardog deployment works behind AWS computes (Amazon Elastic Kubernetes Service (Amazon EKS), Amazon Elastic Container Service (Amazon ECS), and AWS Lambda). We use AgentCore here because it bundles inbound auth, hosting, and tool credentials into one managed service.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Enterprise analytics has been chasing the same goal for two decades: shrink the time between a business question and a trustworthy answer. Scheduled reports gave way to dashboards, then dashboards gave way to self-service business intelligence (BI). Even self-service depended on a data engineer having already built the right model for the right question, and the human analyst remained the bottleneck for everything outside the prepared dataset. Generative AI agents are the next step. Instead of visualizing data, they reason over it. They plan, write queries, evaluate results, refine, and iterate against the company’s live data on demand. &lt;em&gt;Agentic analytics&lt;/em&gt; is the term for this shift: an autonomous agent at every business user’s elbow, doing the analyst’s work without waiting in the request queue.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The hard part is no longer the foundation model (FM). Foundation models available on Amazon Bedrock can already plan multi-step workflows, reason about schemas, and produce SQL well enough to act as a junior analyst would. The hard part sits underneath: the data the model reasons over. Enterprise data is scattered across systems that define the same things differently. The “customer” in your customer relationship management (CRM) system is not the same record as the “customer” in your billing system. “Revenue” calculated by the North American team isn’t the same number the European team would produce. An AI agent given direct access to this fragmented data will write technically valid queries that return wrong, conflicting, or unexplainable answers. Confidence erodes the first time two agents return two different numbers for the same question.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;On AWS, that data sits across a familiar mix. Operational records live in Amazon Aurora and other Amazon Relational Database Service (Amazon RDS) engines. Analytics history lives in Amazon Redshift. Unstructured data lives in Amazon Simple Storage Service (Amazon S3), queried through Amazon Athena, and increasingly in open table formats such as Apache Iceberg, on Amazon S3 Tables, a capability of Amazon S3, which Athena, Amazon EMR, and Amazon Redshift can all read. Each layer is purpose-built for what it stores and most enterprises will keep that shape. The challenge is helping an AI agent reason across all of them at once with the same fluency a senior human analyst would bring to the same question.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Foundation models bring the language and the AWS data application brings the facts. The familiar way to connect them today is &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/what-is/retrieval-augmented-generation/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Retrieval Augmented Generation&lt;/a&gt; (RAG): index policy documents, manuals, and support tickets into Amazon Bedrock Knowledge Bases and pull matching passages into the model’s context at query time. RAG works well when the answer lives in text the search can find. It works less well for analytical questions where the answer depends on joining live records across systems, applying a business rule consistently, and honoring row- or column-level access policies.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;What sits between language and facts, and is usually missing for those analytical questions, is &lt;em&gt;business context and business metrics&lt;/em&gt;. Take a typical retail example: a shared definition of what a &lt;em&gt;customer&lt;/em&gt; is, how an &lt;em&gt;order&lt;/em&gt; links to one, what counts as a &lt;em&gt;big spender&lt;/em&gt; or a &lt;em&gt;high-risk account&lt;/em&gt;, which system owns which fact, and how the numbers the business reports are computed. The same gap shows up in other domains (claims and policies in insurance, patients and encounters in healthcare, parts and shipments in supply chain) under different names. A semantic layer captures that context once and lets every agent and tool reuse it. With it, an AI agent can compose answers from many sources and stand behind the numbers it returns. A semantic layer doesn’t replace RAG. It complements it. Most production systems need both, reachable through the same agent.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;A semantic layer is an ontology-driven view of your enterprise data. The ontology captures the concepts, relationships, attributes and rules that matter to your business. Mappings declare how those concepts are mapped from rows in each live source. The agent queries the layer. The layer translates each query into SQL against the underlying systems at runtime. Data stays where it is. Meaning is captured once and reused.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When that semantic layer is implemented as Stardog does it (an ontology, stable identifiers for every entity, and rules that derive new facts, and constraints that validate the data against the ontology) the result is a &lt;em&gt;knowledge graph&lt;/em&gt;. The data is connected as a graph of business entities rather than rows in tables, every entity has a stable URL-style unique identifier called an &lt;a href="https://www.w3.org/TR/rdf11-concepts/#section-IRIs" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;IRI&lt;/a&gt;, and queries traverse those connections in a W3C-defined, standards-based query language called &lt;a href="https://docs.stardog.com/query-stardog/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;SPARQL&lt;/a&gt;. Two more terms come up repeatedly in the rest of the post:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;A &lt;em&gt;named graph&lt;/em&gt; is a labeled subset of the graph, identified by an IRI of its own. Stardog uses named graphs as the unit of access control. The same query produces different results for different roles depending on which named graphs each role is allowed to read.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;A &lt;em&gt;virtual graph&lt;/em&gt; is a named graph whose contents are not stored in Stardog at all. They live in an external system (Aurora, Amazon Redshift, Athena) and Stardog fetches the rows on demand using the mappings. Federation in this post is implemented as a set of virtual graphs, one per source.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The &lt;a href="https://docs.stardog.com/additional-resources/glossary" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Stardog glossary&lt;/a&gt; defines each of these terms, and the &lt;a href="https://docs.stardog.com/getting-started-series" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Stardog Getting Started series&lt;/a&gt; puts them in context.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;By the end of this post you will know:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;How a semantic layer fits alongside RAG, and when to pick each.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;How to federate Stardog across Amazon Aurora and Amazon Redshift, with notes on extending to Amazon Athena and other AWS data sources.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Why we recommend AgentCore Runtime, Gateway, and Identity for running the agent in production.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Two integration paths from the agent to Stardog: a direct &lt;a href="https://docs.stardog.com/query-stardog/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;SPARQL&lt;/a&gt; tool, and the Stardog Cloud Model Context Protocol (MCP) server as a Gateway tool target.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Tradeoffs on governance, deployment, and what is generally available (GA) versus beta today.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="three-layers-an-agent-needs"&gt;Three layers an agent needs&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;An AI agent that gives reliable, business-context-aware answers depends on three things working together. Each one solves a problem the others can’t.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;1. &lt;strong&gt;Model layer.&lt;/strong&gt; A foundation model that can plan and write. Amazon Bedrock provides a single API to multiple model families. We use Anthropic Claude Sonnet 4.6. The model knows language, it doesn’t know your business.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;2. &lt;strong&gt;Meaning layer.&lt;/strong&gt; A semantic layer that gives the model trustworthy, governed access to the data behind your business questions. The ontology declares the concepts and the rules that derive new facts from them, and the federation pulls live rows from each source at query time. The layer does the work: it figures out which data is relevant, rewrites the SPARQL query into SQL for each source, and joins the rows on shared identifiers. The model has a narrower job. It reads the user’s question, calls the layer when it needs data, and writes the answer in plain English. The actual reasoning over data, like applying the &lt;code&gt;Big_Spender&lt;/code&gt; rule across the federated sources, stays inside Stardog rather than inside the prompt. We use Stardog’s federated knowledge graph over Aurora and Amazon Redshift for this.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;3. &lt;strong&gt;Agent runtime layer.&lt;/strong&gt; The compute that hosts the agent, terminates inbound requests, manages tool credentials, and provides the operational surface for security and governance. On AWS you have a spectrum of choices: managed runtimes like Amazon Bedrock AgentCore at one end, and self-managed options like Amazon ECS, Amazon EKS, or AWS Lambda at the other. The right choice depends on how much of the agent operations you want to own. We use Amazon Bedrock AgentCore in this post because it is the most prescriptive of the options for production agents on AWS.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Of the three, the meaning layer is the gap this post is about, and the rest of the walkthrough builds it. The agent runtime layer is the one most teams underestimate: how does the agent get called, how does it authenticate, where does its credential to the semantic layer live, and how does it scale. AgentCore packages answers to those questions into one managed service, which is why we use it here.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="example-use-case-a-customer-360-agent-across-aurora-and-redshift"&gt;Example use case: A customer 360 agent across Aurora and Amazon Redshift&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;We chose customer 360 (C360) for the rest of this walkthrough because it surfaces every gap an AI agent runs into the moment it tries to do real analytical work, and it lets us show how the semantic layer closes each one in a few hundred lines of mappings and rules. In a typical retail setup, customer profile, address, credit card, and rewards information sit in an operational database. Orders, products, categories, and vendors live in a data warehouse for analytics. Each side is well-tuned for what it does. Neither side, on its own, can answer a question about the customer as a whole. An agent that tries to answer “who are our most valuable customers, and what are they buying?” has to reconcile two databases, two schemas, two definitions of “customer”, and a derived idea (“most valuable”) that lives in nobody’s column. C360 makes those gaps concrete.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The C360 agent runs for the analytics team. It accepts a question in plain English from a sales lead, a marketer, or a fraud analyst. It writes the queries, runs them across the company’s data, and replies with a short narrative answer plus the supporting numbers. The user does not see SQL or SPARQL, and the agent does not see the data it is not allowed to see.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;A user asks the C360 agent, in plain English: &lt;em&gt;“Who are our top spenders in Wisconsin?”&lt;/em&gt; Customer profile and address live in Aurora. Order totals live in Amazon Redshift. The agent has to join them on a stable customer identifier, and that join is one of three things a semantic layer adds:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;1. &lt;strong&gt;Joins across systems through shared meaning.&lt;/strong&gt; The semantic layer maps customer records from both Aurora and Amazon Redshift to a common customer identity using shared business keys. This lets the join be expressed through the semantic model rather than a physical integration pipeline. Without it, that join often requires a maintained pipeline that materializes a third copy of the data and must be kept in sync each time either source changes.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;2. &lt;strong&gt;Derived facts as rules, not queries.&lt;/strong&gt; A definition like “big spender” can live in the ontology as a rule rather than being re-expressed in every query, and every query that mentions it gets the same answer. Without it, that definition gets duplicated across dashboards, notebooks, and reports. When the threshold changes, the copies drift apart and the same question starts returning different answers.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;3. &lt;strong&gt;Graph-level access control.&lt;/strong&gt; Named graph security controls access at the graph level, so different roles see different subsets of the enterprise knowledge graph. For more fine-grained protection, you can designate sensitive properties such as &lt;code&gt;:ssn&lt;/code&gt; and &lt;code&gt;:cardNumber&lt;/code&gt; as protected properties. Users with permission see the actual values, while others see masked values by default. With this approach, different roles can run the same query while the knowledge graph enforces consistent security policies across every application that accesses it.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;We took Stardog’s &lt;a href="https://github.com/stardog-union/knowledge-kits/tree/main/examples/c360" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;C360 Knowledge Kit&lt;/a&gt;, which originally loads local CSVs, and adapted it to federate across Aurora PostgreSQL (customer side) and Amazon Redshift (purchase side). The kit ships an ontology, sample data, and ready-made queries. You can use it as the starting point for your own work.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="the-data-model-the-agent-does-not-see"&gt;The data model the agent does not see&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Underneath the agent, the C360 data is split across two AWS databases. Aurora holds the operational, customer-facing tables. Amazon Redshift holds the analytics fact table and product dimensions.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Aurora PostgreSQL (operational)&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table border="1px" width="100%" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Table&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Key columns&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;customer&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;cid, first_name, last_name, email, ssn, phone, location&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;address&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;id, city, state, zip, street_name&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;credit_card&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;id, cid, card_num, card_type&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;rewards_account&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;id, cid, account_id, create_date&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Amazon Redshift (analytics)&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table border="1px" width="100%" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Table&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Key columns&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;purchase&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;id, cid, pid, date, quantity, price, card&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;product&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;id, name, brand, price, dept&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;category&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;id, dept_name, parent&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;code&gt;vendor&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;id, vendor_name, industry&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The shared identifier across the two databases is the integer &lt;code&gt;cid&lt;/code&gt;. It appears as a primary key in the &lt;code&gt;customer&lt;/code&gt; table on Aurora and as a non-foreign-key column in the &lt;code&gt;purchase&lt;/code&gt; table on Amazon Redshift. There’s no SQL constraint linking them. There can’t be: they live in different engines. Answers that join customers to their orders must reconcile that integer at query time, every time, without ETL.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This is what the semantic layer absorbs. The ontology declares one concept, &lt;code&gt;:Customer&lt;/code&gt;, and one stable identity for it: an &lt;a href="https://www.w3.org/TR/rdf11-concepts/#section-IRIs" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;IRI&lt;/a&gt; of the form &lt;code&gt;urn:stardog:demos:c360:customer:{cid}&lt;/code&gt;. Both the Aurora &lt;code&gt;customer&lt;/code&gt; mapping and the Amazon Redshift &lt;code&gt;purchase&lt;/code&gt; mapping mint that same IRI from their respective &lt;code&gt;cid&lt;/code&gt; values. The agent sees one customer entity with a profile, addresses, cards, rewards accounts, and orders. The warehouses see their own rows, unchanged. The mappings mint the same customer IRI from the &lt;code&gt;cid&lt;/code&gt; in both databases. Stardog uses that shared identity to join the federated results without requiring the underlying databases to know about one another.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="reference-architecture"&gt;Reference architecture&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/09/ML-21021-1-1.png" alt="Reference architecture: a client calls the agent through AgentCore Gateway and Runtime, which queries the semantic layer that federates to Aurora and Redshift" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Figure 1.&lt;/strong&gt; Key point: data stays in Aurora and Amazon Redshift. Only queries flow through the semantic layer.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This is the recommended production architecture. The proof of concept (POC) for this post used Path A with the agent running as a standalone script.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The diagram shows two flows.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Inbound.&lt;/strong&gt; A client (an application, another agent, or Stardog Studio) calls the agent through AgentCore Gateway. Gateway validates the inbound JSON Web Token (JWT) and routes the call to AgentCore Runtime, which hosts the Strands agent. The agent invokes Claude Sonnet 4.6 on Amazon Bedrock for planning and answer composition.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Outbound to the semantic layer.&lt;/strong&gt; Two paths are shown.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;em&gt;Path A (today, orange dashed):&lt;/em&gt; The Strands agent calls a &lt;code&gt;query_kg&lt;/code&gt; SPARQL tool that talks to Stardog directly. Use this when you don’t yet have Voicebox API access (described later in this post).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;em&gt;Path B (Stardog Cloud MCP, when API access is available, blue):&lt;/em&gt; AgentCore Gateway has the Stardog Cloud MCP server registered as an MCP target. Gateway pulls the Stardog token from AgentCore Identity and forwards the call. The agent doesn’t touch the credential.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Stardog itself federates to the warehouses at query time over Java Database Connectivity (JDBC). SPARQL is rewritten to SQL per source. Results are joined inside Stardog on shared IRIs. There is no ETL job and no third copy of the data.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="build-the-semantic-layer"&gt;Build the semantic layer&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Three pieces make the federation work. The ontology declares the concepts (&lt;code&gt;:Customer&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;:Order&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;:Product&lt;/code&gt;) and the relationships between them. The mappings declare how rows in each datastore become instances of those concepts. Reasoning rules derive new facts from the data already in scope. Stardog Designer is the authoring surface for all three.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="the-ontology"&gt;The ontology&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/09/ML-21021-2-1.png" alt="C360 ontology in Stardog Designer showing Customer, Order, Product, and Address concepts connected by labeled relationships, with derived classes such as Big Spender and Large Order" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Figure 2.&lt;/strong&gt; The C360 model in Stardog Designer. Concepts like&lt;/em&gt; Customer&lt;em&gt;,&lt;/em&gt; Order&lt;em&gt;,&lt;/em&gt; Product&lt;em&gt;, and&lt;/em&gt; Address &lt;em&gt;appear as colored nodes connected by labeled relationships (&lt;/em&gt;purchased&lt;em&gt;,&lt;/em&gt; card holder&lt;em&gt;,&lt;/em&gt; has address&lt;em&gt;,&lt;/em&gt; in category&lt;em&gt;). The lighter-shaded nodes around the edges (&lt;/em&gt;Big Spender&lt;em&gt;,&lt;/em&gt; Large Order&lt;em&gt;,&lt;/em&gt; 2022 Orderer&lt;em&gt;, the&lt;/em&gt; Sports Category &lt;em&gt;shopper) are derived classes inferred by reasoning rules in the model rather than columns in any source.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The C360 ontology is authored in &lt;a href="https://docs.stardog.com/stardog-applications/designer/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Stardog Designer&lt;/a&gt;, a no-code visual modeling and mapping tool. The modeler creates concepts (&lt;code&gt;:Customer&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;:Order&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;:Product&lt;/code&gt;), connects them with relationships (&lt;code&gt;:purchasedBy&lt;/code&gt; links an &lt;code&gt;:Order&lt;/code&gt; back to a &lt;code&gt;:Customer&lt;/code&gt;), and declares fields like &lt;code&gt;:ssn&lt;/code&gt; and &lt;code&gt;:cardNumber&lt;/code&gt; as sensitive properties. No OWL or RDF is written by hand. The full C360 model defines roughly ten classes and thirty properties. Designer can also generate a starting model from existing source metadata such as table schemas, &lt;a href="https://hl7.org/fhir/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;FHIR&lt;/a&gt; definitions, use case description and competency questions, which is a faster path than writing the model from scratch.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Portability is a key advantage of Designer which uses &lt;a href="https://www.w3.org/TR/turtle/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Turtle&lt;/a&gt;, the W3C standard serialization for &lt;a href="https://www.w3.org/RDF/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;RDF&lt;/a&gt; to persist models. This standards-based approach helps prevent vendor lock-in and supports interoperability with the broader RDF network:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Import ontologies directly from tools like &lt;a href="https://protege.stanford.edu/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Protégé&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Export data to services such as &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/neptune/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Neptune&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Perform round-trip workflows across other RDF compatible utilities.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;The ontology grows over time as the questions it needs to answer get more specific.&lt;/strong&gt; Most teams start with a small use case and competency questions used to build a model and then it is extended incrementally.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Under the hood: what Designer generates.&lt;/strong&gt; The portion of the model the federation rests on is small (prefix declarations elided for brevity).&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;pre class="turtle"&gt;&lt;code&gt;:Customer a owl:Class .

:purchasedBy a owl:ObjectProperty ;
    so:domainIncludes :Order ;
    so:rangeIncludes :Customer .

:ssn a owl:DatatypeProperty, m:SensitiveProperty ;
    so:domainIncludes :Customer .&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The points worth noticing: every concept gets a stable identifier, the relationship from &lt;code&gt;:Order&lt;/code&gt; to &lt;code&gt;:Customer&lt;/code&gt; is declared once and reused everywhere, and &lt;code&gt;:ssn&lt;/code&gt; is classified as &lt;code&gt;m:SensitiveProperty&lt;/code&gt; (from Stardog’s metadata vocabulary) in the model itself rather than in a downstream policy file. The full ontology is in the &lt;a href="https://github.com/stardog-union/knowledge-kits/tree/main/examples/c360" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;C360 Knowledge Kit&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="the-mappings-federation-building-block"&gt;The mappings (federation building block)&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/09/ML-21021-3-1.png" alt="Stardog Designer mapping editor binding the Customer source to the Customer concept, with the id column set as the primary identifier" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Figure 3.&lt;/strong&gt; The mapping editor in Stardog Designer. The&lt;/em&gt; Customer &lt;em&gt;source on the right is bound to the&lt;/em&gt; Customer &lt;em&gt;concept on the left. The&lt;/em&gt; id &lt;em&gt;column is set as the Primary Identifier, which is what produces the stable IRI used everywhere else. Below the identifier, relationships like&lt;/em&gt; Has Loyalty Program Status &lt;em&gt;and&lt;/em&gt; Interacts With &lt;em&gt;link the customer to other concepts in the same model.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In Designer, mappings are authored visually. The modeler maps the Aurora &lt;code&gt;customer&lt;/code&gt; table to the &lt;code&gt;:Customer&lt;/code&gt; concept and tells Designer that &lt;code&gt;id&lt;/code&gt; is the entity key. Designer does the same on the Amazon Redshift side: map the &lt;code&gt;purchase&lt;/code&gt; table, mark &lt;code&gt;cid&lt;/code&gt; as the customer key. (In database terms, &lt;code&gt;cid&lt;/code&gt; in &lt;code&gt;:purchase&lt;/code&gt; references &lt;code&gt;id&lt;/code&gt; in &lt;code&gt;:Customer&lt;/code&gt;.) The same map-and-mark-the-key flow works for both warehouses. The mappings stay independent, but they line up because the IRI produced from the values in the &lt;code&gt;cid&lt;/code&gt; in &lt;code&gt;:Purchase&lt;/code&gt; is the same IRI as produced for the values of &lt;code&gt;id&lt;/code&gt; in &lt;code&gt;:customer&lt;/code&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The architectural property to understand is the &lt;em&gt;IRI template&lt;/em&gt;. It is what lets the agent answer cross-warehouse questions without ETL. Both mappings produce the same identifier for the same customer. A customer with &lt;code&gt;cid=42&lt;/code&gt; becomes &lt;code&gt;urn:stardog:demos:c360:customer:42&lt;/code&gt; whether the row came from the Aurora &lt;code&gt;customer&lt;/code&gt; table or the Amazon Redshift &lt;code&gt;purchase&lt;/code&gt; table. The agent sees one entity. The warehouses are unchanged. No SQL JOIN ever crosses the two warehouses. The “join” is the IRI agreement.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Under the hood: what Designer generates.&lt;/strong&gt; When Designer saves a mapping, it emits &lt;a href="https://docs.stardog.com/virtual-graphs/mapping-data-sources" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Stardog Mapping Syntax (SMS)&lt;/a&gt;, a superset of the W3C R2RML standard that uses a SPARQL-like syntax. Stardog reads R2RML directly and can export SMS back to R2RML, so mappings port either direction. The two excerpts that carry the federation building block look like this:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;pre class="sparql"&gt;&lt;code&gt;# Aurora customer mapping (excerpt)
BIND(TEMPLATE("urn:stardog:demos:c360:customer:{cid}") AS ?iri)

# Redshift order mapping (excerpt)
BIND(TEMPLATE("urn:stardog:demos:c360:customer:{cid}") AS ?cust_iri)&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The full mapping files for every C360 table are in the &lt;a href="https://github.com/stardog-union/knowledge-kits/tree/main/examples/c360" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;kit&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following diagram traces a question from the agent down to the data sources and back. The middle band is the semantic layer. Nothing in it stores rows. The rows live in Aurora and Amazon Redshift. Stardog uses the ontology and the mappings to decide which SQL to run where, then joins the result sets on the shared IRI before handing them back.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/09/ML-21021-4-1.png" alt="Query flow from the C360 agent to Aurora and Redshift, with SPARQL rewritten to SQL per source and result sets joined on the shared customer IRI" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Figure 4.&lt;/strong&gt; The query flow from the C360 agent to Aurora and Amazon Redshift and back. SPARQL is rewritten to SQL per source. Result sets are joined inside Stardog on the shared customer IRI.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="reasoning-over-the-graph"&gt;Reasoning over the graph&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Stardog supports &lt;a href="https://docs.stardog.com/inference-engine/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;OWL&lt;/a&gt; reasoning and user-defined rules written in Stardog Rule Syntax (SRS) or &lt;a href="https://docs.stardog.com/inference-engine/user-defined-rules" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;SWRL&lt;/a&gt;. &lt;a href="https://docs.stardog.com/inference-engine/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Reasoning&lt;/a&gt; is the process of inferring new facts from existing data using the schema. The kit defines derived classes such as &lt;code&gt;Big_Spender&lt;/code&gt; (a customer who has placed at least one order above a threshold) and &lt;code&gt;Large_Order&lt;/code&gt;. With reasoning enabled at query time, asking “how many big spenders by state?” works without precomputing or materializing those facts. The rules are defined once in the semantic model and evaluated over live data when the query runs.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Encoding business definitions once, instead of re-implementing them in SQL queries, dashboards and prompts, is one of the hardest parts of moving beyond RAG.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="govern-access-with-named-graph-security"&gt;Govern access with named-graph security&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Customer 360 data is regulated. Two requirements show up early.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;1. Some users (HR, fraud) need to see personally identifiable information (PII) like a Social Security number (SSN) and full card number. Most users (marketing, analytics) must not.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;2. The control shouldn’t require changes in the underlying warehouses.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Stardog’s mechanism for this is &lt;a href="https://docs.stardog.com/operating-stardog/security/fine-grained-security" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;named-graph security&lt;/a&gt;. A named graph is a labeled subset of the data. Named-graph security gates reads against each named graph on a per-user basis. Each virtual graph in the federation has its own named-graph IRI. We split the Aurora mapping into two virtual graphs.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;code&gt;aurora_c360_safe&lt;/code&gt;: Customer profile minus PII (everything except &lt;code&gt;:ssn&lt;/code&gt; and &lt;code&gt;:cardNumber&lt;/code&gt;).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;code&gt;aurora_c360_pii&lt;/code&gt;: Only the PII triples, keyed to the same customer IRI.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The Amazon Redshift mapping is one graph, &lt;code&gt;redshift_c360&lt;/code&gt;. We then create two roles.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;code&gt;hr_user&lt;/code&gt;: Read on all three virtual graphs.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;code&gt;marketing_user&lt;/code&gt;: Read on &lt;code&gt;aurora_c360_safe&lt;/code&gt; and &lt;code&gt;redshift_c360&lt;/code&gt; only.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When &lt;code&gt;marketing_user&lt;/code&gt; runs a SPARQL query that asks for &lt;code&gt;?c :ssn ?ssn&lt;/code&gt;, Stardog’s planner treats &lt;code&gt;aurora_c360_pii&lt;/code&gt; as if it did not exist for that user. The JDBC pushdown for the SSN column doesn’t happen. No application-level filter, no warehouse-side change, no chance of leaking the value through a misconfigured BI tool. Same SPARQL query, two roles, two different result sets.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For row-level filters (a US-East analyst only sees east-coast customers, for example) the same named-graph mechanism scales: split the source into more virtual graphs along the row dimension, then grant per-graph reads. This is repetition of the PII pattern, not a new feature.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Stardog also supports tagging individual properties as sensitive on the ontology, with the tag driving column-level masking at query time. This is worth evaluating as an alternative to the preceding named-graph split. It fits best when sensitive fields are scattered across many entities and you would rather mark them in the ontology than partition the data. See &lt;a href="https://docs.stardog.com/operating-stardog/security/fine-grained-security" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Stardog’s fine-grained security docs&lt;/a&gt; for the configuration steps.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="run-the-agent-and-call-stardog-from-it"&gt;Run the agent and call Stardog from it&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Strands Agents is a small framework for building agents. A short Python script wires Claude Sonnet 4.6 (on Amazon Bedrock) to a tool that runs SPARQL against Stardog. A summary of the ontology is loaded into the agent’s system prompt. The model writes SPARQL, the tool returns rows, the model summarizes the results.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;That code is portable. The same agent can run as a Python script on a laptop, in a container on Amazon ECS or Amazon EKS, behind an Application Load Balancer, or as an AWS Lambda function. For production we recommend deploying it on Amazon Bedrock AgentCore because it bundles the operational scaffolding into one managed service.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;AgentCore Runtime&lt;/strong&gt; hosts the agent and handles concurrency and session state.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;AgentCore Gateway&lt;/strong&gt; is the inbound surface that validates the JWT from your identity provider (IdP) and routes the call to Runtime.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;AgentCore Identity&lt;/strong&gt; holds the Stardog token as a credential provider, so the credential doesn’t live in the agent code.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;If you pick a different runtime, the same building blocks still need a home: a hosting layer, an inbound authenticator, and a credential vault. The integration patterns that follow don’t change. What changes is which AWS service holds each piece.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The integration to Stardog is where deployments differ. Two paths are worth knowing. Default to Path A unless you already have Voicebox API access.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Path A: Direct SPARQL tool.&lt;/strong&gt; A small Python tool wraps the &lt;code&gt;pystardog&lt;/code&gt; client and is exposed to the agent through Strands’ tool decorator. The agent writes SPARQL, the tool runs it, the tool returns rows. This works on Stardog instances you can reach over HTTPS, including Stardog Cloud’s Free tier. We used this path in the POC because it requires no special enablement on the Stardog account.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Path B: Stardog Cloud MCP server as a Gateway tool target.&lt;/strong&gt; Stardog publishes an official MCP server, &lt;a href="https://github.com/stardog-union/stardog-cloud-mcp" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;stardog-union/stardog-cloud-mcp&lt;/a&gt;, that exposes &lt;a href="https://www.stardog.com/voicebox/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Voicebox&lt;/a&gt; (Stardog’s natural-language query layer) as three tools: &lt;code&gt;voicebox_ask&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;voicebox_generate_query&lt;/code&gt;, and &lt;code&gt;voicebox_settings&lt;/code&gt;. Voicebox runs its own ontology-aware natural-language-to-SPARQL pipeline and returns the reasoning chain and provenance with each answer. AgentCore Gateway can register this MCP server as a tool target, which means the agent inside Runtime sees Voicebox as a tool with no glue code, and AgentCore Identity injects the token at the Gateway hop.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The MCP server’s local mode is stable. Remote mode is in beta. Path B also requires a Voicebox API token, which is gated to specific accounts. Contact Stardog if your portal doesn’t show “Manage API Keys.”&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Pick Path A when you don’t yet have API access or when you want full control over SPARQL generation. Pick Path B when you have API access and want Voicebox’s reasoning chain and provenance for no additional cost.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;We recommend enabling Amazon Bedrock prompt caching for production deployments.&lt;/strong&gt; The agent reuses the same large system prompt, the C360 ontology summary, on every call. &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/prompt-caching.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock prompt caching&lt;/a&gt; keeps the cached portion of that prompt warm across calls, which reduces input-token cost on repeat invocations. In Stardog deployments, prompt caching can reduce per-query input-token cost when the same ontology context is reused across requests. Caching is configured per request. Check your agent framework’s &lt;code&gt;BedrockModel&lt;/code&gt; parameters for how to expose the cache point.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="sample-queries-and-verification"&gt;Sample queries and verification&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To confirm the federation behaved as designed, we ran three categories of questions against the live deployment.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Aurora-only.&lt;/strong&gt; &lt;em&gt;“List ten customers and their state.”&lt;/em&gt; Source data is in Aurora. Stardog issues one SQL query against the Aurora &lt;code&gt;customer&lt;/code&gt; and &lt;code&gt;address&lt;/code&gt; tables and returns the rows. The Amazon Redshift virtual graph is not touched.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Amazon Redshift-only.&lt;/strong&gt; &lt;em&gt;“Top ten products by units sold.”&lt;/em&gt; Source data is in Amazon Redshift. Stardog issues a single grouped aggregation against the &lt;code&gt;purchase&lt;/code&gt; table and returns the totals. Aurora isn’t touched.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Federated.&lt;/strong&gt; &lt;em&gt;“Top ten customer spenders, with their state.”&lt;/em&gt; This needs Aurora customers and addresses joined with Amazon Redshift orders on the shared customer IRI. The actual SPARQL the agent runs:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;pre class="sparql"&gt;&lt;code&gt;PREFIX : &amp;lt;tag:stardog:api:ecomm:&amp;gt;

SELECT ?cust ?fname ?lname ?state (SUM(?price * ?qty) AS ?spend)
WHERE {
    GRAPH &amp;lt;virtual://aurora_c360_safe&amp;gt; {
        ?cust a :Customer ;
            :firstName ?fname ;
            :lastName ?lname ;
            :address ?a .
        ?a :state ?state .
    }
    GRAPH &amp;lt;virtual://redshift_c360&amp;gt; {
        ?o :purchasedBy ?cust ;
            :purchasePrice ?price ;
            :quantity ?qty .
    }
}
GROUP BY ?cust ?fname ?lname ?state
ORDER BY DESC(?spend)
LIMIT 10&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Stardog issues a SQL query to each source (one against the Aurora &lt;code&gt;customer&lt;/code&gt; and &lt;code&gt;address&lt;/code&gt; tables, one that aggregates Amazon Redshift &lt;code&gt;purchase&lt;/code&gt; by &lt;code&gt;cid&lt;/code&gt;), receives both result sets, and joins them on the &lt;code&gt;?cust&lt;/code&gt; variable, that is, the IRI mapped from &lt;code&gt;cid&lt;/code&gt; on each side. The top result for our seed data was Carolyn Inworth, $502,529 in total spend across her purchases. The same query against the same data returns the same number on every run.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;We then ran the reasoning case: &lt;em&gt;“how many big spenders are there per state?”&lt;/em&gt; with reasoning enabled. Stardog applied the &lt;code&gt;Big_Spender&lt;/code&gt; rule across the federated graph at query time and returned counts per state without precomputing or materializing the derived class.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For governance we ran the same SPARQL query twice, once as &lt;code&gt;hr_user&lt;/code&gt; and once as &lt;code&gt;marketing_user&lt;/code&gt;. The HR role saw the SSN value bound. The marketing role got back the same row shape with an empty &lt;code&gt;?ssn&lt;/code&gt; binding, with no change to the query text and no warehouse-side filter.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="extending-the-semantic-layer-across-aws-data-sources-and-beyond"&gt;Extending the semantic layer across AWS data sources and beyond&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Stardog’s virtual graph mechanism is not specific to Aurora or Amazon Redshift. To add a new source or extend the ontology, the modeler works in Stardog Designer, the same authoring surface used for the initial C360 model. Designer generates the SMS mapping. When the modeler marks the same entity key (for example, &lt;code&gt;cid&lt;/code&gt; for the customer concept), the IRI template matches and the new source becomes part of the federated semantic layer without changing the ontology or existing queries. On AWS this includes Amazon Athena (data lake on Amazon S3) and Amazon RDS engines that ship a JDBC driver. Off AWS this includes Snowflake, Google BigQuery, MongoDB, and SAP HANA. The full list is in Stardog’s &lt;a href="https://docs.stardog.com/virtual-graphs/data-sources/supported-data-sources" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;supported data sources&lt;/a&gt; page.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The architectural property to preserve as you add sources is the IRI template. As long as the new mappings mint IRIs that match existing ones for the same logical entity, the new source can participate naturally in the existing federated graph. Stardog calls this “cooperation without coordination”: each team owns its source and its mappings, and the graph composes.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="stardog-cloud-compared-to-self-managed-on-amazon-eks"&gt;Stardog Cloud compared to self-managed on Amazon EKS&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Stardog runs in two shapes on AWS, and the choice is operational, not functional.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Stardog Cloud (SaaS).&lt;/strong&gt; Stardog operates the SOC 2 compliant service with 99.9 percent service level agreements (SLAs). You bring data sources and credentials. Free, Essentials, and Enterprise tiers exist. Use this when you want to skip operations, when your data sources are reachable from Stardog Cloud (over the public internet, or through AWS PrivateLink for Enterprise), and when the Stardog-managed update cadence works for you. We used Stardog Cloud for the work behind this post.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Self-managed on Amazon EKS.&lt;/strong&gt; Stardog ships container images and Helm charts. You run the cluster, manage upgrades, and handle backup. Use this when you need to keep data plane traffic inside your virtual private cloud (VPC), when you want to control the version cadence, when compliance requires a specific deployment posture, or when the data sources are themselves only reachable from inside your VPC.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Egress matters either way. Stardog Cloud connects to Aurora and Amazon Redshift over JDBC, so the security groups on those data sources must allow inbound connections from Stardog Cloud’s egress IP range. Stardog publishes the current set of egress IPs in their documentation. Allowlist those CIDRs on each data source security group and the federation works. See &lt;a href="https://docs.stardog.com/stardog-cloud/advanced-topics#white-listing-virtual-graph-calls-from-stardog-cloud" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Allow-listing virtual graph calls from Stardog Cloud&lt;/a&gt; for the current values and the configuration steps.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Reliable agentic analytics needs three things working together: a foundation model that can plan and generate queries, a semantic layer that provides a governed business view across live enterprise data, and a production runtime that hosts the agent and manages security. Amazon Bedrock, Stardog, and Amazon Bedrock AgentCore each play one of those roles.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The key architectural idea is that the agent talks to the semantic layer, not directly to the underlying databases. As data sources and schemas evolve, the ontology and mappings absorb those changes, allowing agents to continue reasoning over stable business concepts rather than physical tables and columns.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="where-to-start"&gt;Where to start&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Most teams that get this working start with one workflow rather than a wide rollout.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;A practical sequence:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;1. &lt;strong&gt;Pick one workflow&lt;/strong&gt; that gets answered slowly today because the data spans two or three systems. Narrower is better.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;2. &lt;strong&gt;Write three to five competency questions&lt;/strong&gt; the workflow needs answered. These are the queries the semantic layer has to support on day one.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;3. &lt;strong&gt;Bootstrap the ontology&lt;/strong&gt; from your source schemas in Designer, then refine it against the competency questions until each one returns a sensible answer.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;4. &lt;strong&gt;Stand up the federation&lt;/strong&gt; as virtual graphs over the live sources. No ETL, no third copy.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;5. &lt;strong&gt;Wire the agent&lt;/strong&gt; to the layer through Path A or Path B, depending on your Voicebox API access.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The &lt;a href="https://github.com/stardog-union/knowledge-kits/tree/main/examples/c360" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Stardog C360 Knowledge Kit&lt;/a&gt; is a useful starting point. It is the same kit this post adapts.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="further-reading"&gt;Further reading&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Stardog on knowledge graphs for enterprise AI: &lt;a href="https://www.stardog.com/blog/what-llms-dont-know/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;What LLMs don’t know&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href="https://www.stardog.com/blog/knowledge-graphs-are-critical-to-trustworthy-enterprise-ai/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Knowledge graphs are critical to trustworthy enterprise AI&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Stardog data source coverage: &lt;a href="https://docs.stardog.com/virtual-graphs/data-sources/supported-data-sources" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Stardog supported data sources&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt;

		<item>
		<title>Scaling agentic workflows with native case management in Amazon Quick Automate</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/scaling-agentic-workflows-with-native-case-management-in-amazon-quick-automate/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Sumit Wasuja]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Fri, 10 Jul 2026 15:28:54 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Advanced (300)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Quick Sight]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Quick Suite]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">54d76cceb6bd0af8db3a440ce3ba321b220e8a79</guid>

					<description>In this post, we show you how to combine case management with agentic automation capabilities in Quick Automate. We introduce case management and explore the lifecycle of cases in an agentic workflow from case creation through processing to resolution. We cover how to create and manage single or multiple cases, automatically track and update status, handle exceptions, and incorporate Human-in-the-loop (HITL) steps within workflows. We also show the case creator-processor pattern that enables dynamic scaling. Finally, we walk through how to structure case management for enterprise processes, including HITL and case tracking, through a real-life use case.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;An artificial intelligence (AI) agent can process an invoice, help adjudicate a claim, or classify a support ticket in a proof of concept. But running these agents across thousands or even millions of work items in a production environment introduces an entirely different set of challenges. At enterprise scale, success depends on much more than the agent itself. Organizations need to track the state of each work item as it moves through multiple agents and systems, surface exactly where failures occur and why, allow a human to step in when needed, and scale the infrastructure dynamically based on demand. &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/quick/automate/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Quick Automate&lt;/a&gt; addresses these operational challenges through &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quick/latest/userguide/actions-orchestration.html#case-management" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;case management&lt;/a&gt;. Every work item is represented as a case that persists throughout its lifecycle, providing step-by-step visibility into workflow state, supporting human-in-the-loop processing where judgment is needed, and enabling parallel execution so teams can orchestrate agents to automate business processes at scale.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, we show you how to combine case management with agentic automation capabilities in &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/quick/automate/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Quick Automate&lt;/a&gt;. We introduce case management and explore the lifecycle of cases in an agentic workflow from case creation through processing to resolution. We cover how to create and manage single or multiple cases, automatically track and update status, handle exceptions, and incorporate &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quick/latest/userguide/actions-orchestration.html#human-in-the-loop" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Human-in-the-loop (HITL)&lt;/a&gt; steps within workflows. We also show the case creator-processor pattern that enables dynamic scaling. Finally, we walk through how to structure case management for enterprise processes, including HITL and case tracking, through a real-life use case.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/quick/automate/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Quick Automate&lt;/a&gt; combines AI agents and workflow orchestration within &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/quick/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Quick&lt;/a&gt; to automate complex, end-to-end business processes across applications, UIs, and APIs. By natively combining agentic and deterministic automation capabilities with enterprise workflow orchestration, Quick Automate brings structure, visibility, and control to agent-driven business processes. It provides native case management with trackable work items along with controls like granular access management, activity logging, version control, exception handling, and &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quick/latest/userguide/actions-orchestration.html#human-in-the-loop" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;HITL&lt;/a&gt; capabilities. The result is that processes move reliably from initiation to resolution at enterprise scale.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Case management in Quick Automate delivers several key benefits. It provides improved visibility as workflows progress through clearly defined stages with real-time tracking. Process owners can see exactly where each work item stands, while managers can monitor throughput, surface bottlenecks early, and intervene before delays impact business outcomes. Parallel processing allows multiple cases to be executed concurrently, increasing throughput and helping organizations meet business service level agreements (SLAs). Compliance, auditability, and governance are enhanced because every action, decision, and state transition is recorded as part of the case history. Stakeholder engagement also increases through real-time status updates that keep operators, managers, and business stakeholders aligned. Communication happens within the context of each case, replacing fragmented email threads with centralized collaboration, clear ownership, and fewer lost handoffs.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="prerequisites"&gt;Prerequisites&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Before implementing case management workflows in &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/quick/automate/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Quick Automate&lt;/a&gt;, verify you have access to &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/quick/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Quick&lt;/a&gt; and that you are working in the correct &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/global-infrastructure/latest/regions/aws-regions.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Region&lt;/a&gt; for your organization, because workflows within &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/quick/automate/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Quick Automate&lt;/a&gt; are region-specific. Your user account must have an &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/quick/enterprise/?trk=0ea79374-057c-4897-84f0-5fe792905a8f&amp;amp;sc_channel=ps#get-started" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Enterprise license&lt;/a&gt; to author workflows in &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/quick/automate/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Quick Automate&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="case-lifecycle-and-status"&gt;Case lifecycle and status&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quick/latest/userguide/actions-orchestration.html#case-management" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Case management&lt;/a&gt; in &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/quick/automate/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Quick Automate&lt;/a&gt; transforms complex, long-running workflows into discrete, trackable work units that can be processed in parallel. Each case functions as a discrete work item that progresses through defined lifecycle stages. A case contains a case type, which is a user-defined container for grouping related work such as invoices or claims. It also includes a reference name that serves as a unique ID for tracking, and custom data fields stored as key-value pairs that hold the business information needed for processing. The system automatically manages additional metadata including the status of the case in the workflow, exception details if a failure occurs, and execution logs pertaining to the case. The following table describes the lifecycle statuses a case can have.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table border="1px" width="100%" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Status&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Description&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;How case enters this Status&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;What happens next&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Ready&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Workflow generates new cases available for processing&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Workflow generates a new case&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Workflow consumes the case and begins execution&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;In Progress&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Workflow actively processing the case&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Workflow starts processing a &lt;em&gt;Ready&lt;/em&gt; case&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Case moves to &lt;em&gt;Successful&lt;/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;Failed&lt;/em&gt;, or &lt;em&gt;Pending Resolution&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Successful&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Case has completed each step without errors&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Each step within Process case finished without exceptions&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Terminal state. No further processing occurs.&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Failed&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Case finished with a business or system exception&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;An exception is raised during processing&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Terminal state – check logs for details.&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Pending Resolution&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Case paused, waiting for human task completion&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;A task is created in the Task Center. Case processing is suspended while the system moves on to the next case.&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;When the task is completed in the Task Center, the case automatically transitions back to the &lt;em&gt;Ready&lt;/em&gt; state with the associated human input.&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="case-management-automation-best-practices"&gt;Case management automation best practices&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quick/latest/userguide/actions-orchestration.html#case-management" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Case management&lt;/a&gt; in &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/quick/automate/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Quick Automate&lt;/a&gt; is integrated into the standard automation lifecycle. The following subsections walk through each phase, from initial design through monitoring in production.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="designing-an-automation"&gt;Designing an automation&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When designing your workflow, start by identifying what constitutes a case for your automation. A case represents a single unit of work, such as an invoice, claim, ticket, or order, that moves through the workflow from initiation to resolution.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Next, decide how to create cases. Cases can be created individually as events occur or generated in batches from sources such as files, databases, or external systems. Then determine how to process cases. Cases can be processed sequentially or in parallel. Although a single automation can run the entire workflow, teams often separate responsibilities into two automations to enable parallel processing and clearer ownership. The Case Creator ingests data from sources such as Excel files, databases, and web applications, and transforms each record into a case representing a discrete work item. The Case Processor consumes cases and performs the actual workflow steps, including exception handling and HITL tasks. Multiple processors can run in parallel to increase throughput.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Finally, determine where and when human intervention is needed and incorporate it as part of the workflow. Teams can create human checkpoints that pause a case, present reviewers with relevant data and decision fields, and automatically resume processing after the task is completed.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="authoring-an-automation"&gt;Authoring an automation&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/quick/automate/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Quick Automate&lt;/a&gt; provides builders with two actions for ingesting work.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The first is &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quick/latest/userguide/actions-orchestration.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Create New Case&lt;/a&gt;, used when your data source is an individual event such as a user submitting a form, an API call arriving, or a support ticket being raised. You provide a unique reference name (for example, &lt;code&gt;Order-12345&lt;/code&gt;), select a case type that groups similar cases (such as &lt;code&gt;SalesOrders&lt;/code&gt;), and attach key-value pairs containing the case’s custom data. This action creates exactly one case and immediately sets it to Ready.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The second is &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quick/latest/userguide/actions-orchestration.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Create Multiple Cases&lt;/a&gt;, used when your source is a spreadsheet, CSV file, or table of records such as bulk imports or scheduled data loads. You specify the case type, source table, and which column serves as the unique ID. The action creates a separate case for each row, automatically mapping each column to a custom data field.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Case data often needs modification as workflows progress through different stages. With the update cases action, you can add or modify existing case data by providing a case ID and key-value pairs. Use this action to record processing outcomes, capture audit timestamps, store calculated values, or maintain custom statuses. This action is only available for In Progress cases. Access updated information using &lt;code&gt;updated_case["custom_data"]["key_name"]&lt;/code&gt; in your automation. If you need to retrieve or locate existing cases dynamically, use the Search Cases action. With this action, you can query cases based on filters such as case type, reference ID, status, or other attributes. The returned results can then be used to inspect case details, trigger additional processing, or update cases as part of the workflow.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quick/latest/userguide/actions-orchestration.html#human-in-the-loop" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Human in the loop (HITL) tasks&lt;/a&gt;, builders can use the Create User Task action to pause a case and present a human reviewer with relevant context and decision fields. While the case waits in Pending Resolution status, other cases continue to process. When the reviewer completes the task in &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quick/latest/userguide/hitl-task-center.html#managing-tasks" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Task Center&lt;/a&gt;, the case automatically transitions back to Ready status with the associated human input, and processing resumes. Because the automation resumes from the beginning, it should check the &lt;code&gt;latest_task_resolution&lt;/code&gt; attribute to determine the next branch of execution.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="deploying-an-automation"&gt;Deploying an automation&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;After an automation is authored, you publish and configure it for production execution. You can publish workflows and schedule them to run at defined intervals. During deployment, you configure two key aspects. First, you set the number of parallel instances to run concurrently when configuring the trigger, based on the expected workload. Case processor automations can run multiple instances in parallel to distribute the case volume. Second, for HITL tasks, you specify the users or groups who can resolve tasks in Task Center. During deployment, you add individuals or groups to grant Quick users access to complete tasks.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="monitoring-an-automation"&gt;Monitoring an automation&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/quick/automate/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Quick Automate&lt;/a&gt; provides real-time visibility into each case as it moves through a workflow, allowing users to track status across each processor instance from a single dashboard. The monitoring view shows key metrics such as successful completion rates, business exceptions (caused by logical or data-related issues such as missing required fields), system exceptions (caused by technical failure), and incomplete cases. These metrics help teams quickly identify bottlenecks and measure automation performance. Each case record includes its status, metadata, and processing details, and users can drill into cases to view full logs, custom data fields, exception messages, timestamps, and screenshots where available.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="use-case-automating-multi-bank-statement-processing-with-case-management"&gt;Use case: Automating multi-bank statement processing with case management&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This use case demonstrates how &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/quick/automate/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Quick Automate&lt;/a&gt; reduces manual data entry across two systems using case management for parallel processing. We walk through a real-world treasury consolidation scenario, from solution architecture through implementation details, showing how each concept introduced in the previous sections applies to production automation.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Corporations banking with multiple institutions face a recurring operational challenge. Each month, treasury analysts download statements from each bank’s portal and manually key hundreds of transactions into the company’s central treasury management system. A single data entry error for amount can cascade into reconciliation issues that take hours to trace, and Sarbanes-Oxley (SOX) compliance requires every entry to be accurate and auditable. The manual process is slow, error-prone, and delays month-end reconciliation.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="solution-architecture"&gt;Solution architecture&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following diagram shows the end-to-end solution architecture for the multi-bank statement processing automation. The case creator and processor automations work together to enable parallel processing and reduce cycle times at scale. The first automation, case creator, retrieves the latest bank statement, extracts each transaction using a custom AI agent, and loads them as individual cases. The second automation, case processor, processes each case by navigating to the banking portal, selecting the correct account, and entering the transaction. Multiple processor instances can run simultaneously, each picking up and entering transactions independently. Payments over $200 are flagged for human review, where a treasury analyst verifies the payee, amount, and authorization before approving or discarding the transaction. Rejected transactions are captured as business exceptions with the analyst’s reason code and comments for the audit trail.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/08/ML-20703-1.png" alt="Architecture diagram of the case creator and case processor automations for multi-bank statement processing" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Figure 1: Solution architecture for multi-bank statement processing&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="implementation-creator-processor-design"&gt;Implementation: Creator-processor design&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quick/latest/userguide/building-automations.html#creation-methods" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;case creator&lt;/a&gt; workflow (Treasury Bank Statement Gathering) performs three key activities. It navigates to the bank statement portal and generates the latest statement for download. It then uses a custom AI agent to parse the downloaded PDF and extract each transaction into structured data fields. Finally, it batch-creates cases with one case per extracted transaction, each set to Ready state for downstream processing. The following image shows the case creator workflow as authored in Quick Automate. The workflow contains two process steps. The first handles browser navigation to the bank portal and PDF download, and the second invokes the custom AI agent for extraction followed by batch case creation.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/08/ML-20703-2.png" alt="Case creator workflow in Quick Automate with two process steps for bank portal navigation and AI agent extraction" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Figure 2: Case creator workflow in Quick Automate&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quick/latest/userguide/automate-custom-agents.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Custom agent&lt;/a&gt;, which is a specialized task agent configured with actions and instructions, is used natively within the workflow. It receives the downloaded PDF and uses a Python code tool to extract transaction line items into a structured table. The agent’s output schema defines the fields needed for each case. The following image shows the agent configuration with its instruction and output schema.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/08/ML-20703-3.png" alt="Custom AI agent configuration showing the instruction and output schema for transaction extraction" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Figure 3: Custom AI agent configuration&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;After the agent extracts all transactions, the automation uses the Create Multiple Cases action to batch-create cases. It specifies “Bank Transaction” as the case type, maps the agent’s output table as the custom data source, and designates the Reference column as the unique ID for each case. The following image shows the batch case creation configuration within the workflow.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/08/ML-20703-4.png" alt="Create multiple cases configuration mapping the agent output table to case custom data fields" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Figure 4: Create multiple cases&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;After it is created, each case contains the full transaction data as custom attributes. A case record includes the case status (Ready), its reference name, the case type (Bank Transaction), and custom data fields containing the bank name, account number, transaction date, description, type, and amount. The following image shows the Monitor dashboard with the case table and its key components.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/08/ML-20703-5.png" alt="Monitor dashboard showing created cases with status, reference name, and custom data fields" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Figure 5: Monitor dashboard with created cases&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quick/latest/userguide/building-automations.html#creation-methods" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;case processor&lt;/a&gt; workflow (Treasury Bank Transaction Entry) retrieves cases in Ready status and processes each transaction independently. It first opens the banking portal and authenticates as an administrator. It then enters a processing loop that retrieves cases of type “Bank Transaction” and handles them one at a time. Because Quick Automate supports multiple concurrent processor instances, you can scale this automation horizontally to match transaction volume.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For each case, the processor first checks whether the case has already been through human review. If the case was previously routed to a treasury analyst and has returned with a resolution, the processor checks the authorization outcome. Approved cases proceed directly to transaction entry. Rejected cases are raised as business exceptions with the analyst’s reason code and comments, creating a complete audit record without entering the transaction into the portal.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For cases that have not yet been reviewed, the processor evaluates the payment threshold. If the transaction is a payment (Credit type) and the amount is $200 or more, the case is routed to a treasury analyst for dual-authorization review through the Task Center. The HITL task includes transaction details, an authorization drop-down list with Approved and Rejected options, a reason code drop-down list for rejections, and a free-text comments field. The case enters Pending Resolution status while awaiting the analyst’s decision, and the processor moves on to the next available case without waiting.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following image shows the case processor workflow as authored in Quick Automate, including the conditional logic for human review handling, payment threshold evaluation, HITL task creation, and transaction entry.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/08/ML-20703-6.png" alt="Case processor workflow in Quick Automate with conditional logic for human review, payment threshold, and transaction entry" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Figure 6: Case processor workflow in Quick Automate&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For transactions that do not require human review, or that have been approved by an analyst, the processor uses a &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quick/latest/userguide/ui-agents.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;UI agent&lt;/a&gt; to enter the transaction into the banking portal. The agent navigates to the dashboard, adds a new transaction, selects the appropriate type and amount along with other details, and submits the form. The agent also validates that the transaction entry succeeded. The following image shows the UI agent configuration with its natural language instruction for transaction entry and validation.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/08/ML-20703-7.png" alt="UI agent configuration with a natural language instruction for entering and validating transactions in the banking portal" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Figure 7: Native UI agent and instruction&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="monitoring-case-details-and-status"&gt;Monitoring case details and status&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quick/latest/userguide/monitoring-performance.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Monitor dashboard&lt;/a&gt; provides real-time visibility into case processing performance for the treasury consolidation automation. From this view, operators can track how many cases have been created, how many are currently in progress, and how many have succeeded, failed, or are pending human resolution. The dashboard includes charts showing case creation trends and exception patterns over time, supporting both operational monitoring and compliance audit needs.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following image shows the Cases tab in the Monitor dashboard, displaying case statuses, processing times, and exception details across all processor instances.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/08/ML-20703-8.png" alt="Cases tab in the Monitor dashboard showing case statuses, processing times, and exception details" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Figure 8: Cases&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Operators can also access HITL tasks through the Tasks tab to view which transactions are awaiting analyst review, track SLA adherence, and see the resolution history including approval decisions and rejection reasons. The following image shows the HITL task as it appears in the Task Center. The treasury analyst sees the full transaction context along with the authorization form fields for their review decision.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/08/ML-20703-9.png" alt="HITL task in the Task Center showing transaction context and authorization form fields for analyst review" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Figure 9: Tasks&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quick/latest/userguide/actions-orchestration.html#case-management" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Case management&lt;/a&gt; in &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/quick/automate/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Quick Automate&lt;/a&gt; empowers you to handle high-volume, complex automations at scale. By converting your work into discrete, trackable cases with defined lifecycles, you gain complete operational visibility into workflows. This approach reduces cycle times through parallel processing, improves decision quality by routing critical cases to human reviewers, and builds operational confidence through real-time monitoring that surfaces exceptions before they impact outcomes.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="next-steps"&gt;Next steps&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Start implementing case management in your workflows by reviewing the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quick/latest/userguide/actions-orchestration.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;orchestration documentation for Quick Automate&lt;/a&gt; and trying the automations as pre-built samples as described in this post. Open Quick Automate, navigate to your project, choose Start building, and search for “Treasury Bank Statement Gathering” (case creator) and “Treasury Bank Transaction Entry” (case processor) in the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quick/latest/userguide/building-automations.html#creation-methods" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;sample automation library&lt;/a&gt;. After you open both samples, you can run the case creator first to generate transaction cases, then configure and run the case processor to begin entering transactions in parallel.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You can adapt this pattern to other automations like invoice processing or claims adjudication by modifying the case creation logic and processor routing rules while maintaining the same core orchestration structure. For additional resources, see the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quick/latest/userguide/using-amazon-quick-automate.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;public documentation for Quick Automate&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;hr&gt;
