		<item>
		<title>Disaggregated prefill and decode for LLM inference on SageMaker HyperPod</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/disaggregated-prefill-and-decode-for-llm-inference-on-sagemaker-hyperpod/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Xuan Lu]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Fri, 10 Jul 2026 15:20:16 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Advanced (300)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Elastic Kubernetes Service]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon SageMaker HyperPod]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Amazon Simple Storage Service (S3)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Technical How-to]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">44719c394930a8bd8c11292c18a583053d398cee</guid>

					<description>In this post, we show how to implement DPD with vLLM on Amazon SageMaker HyperPod using the HyperPod Inference Operator.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;When prefill and decode share a GPU, long prompts stall token generation for every concurrent request. Disaggregated Prefill and Decode (DPD) removes this interference by running each phase on separate GPU pools connected through Elastic Fabric Adapter (EFA) with Remote Direct Memory Access (RDMA). Large language model (LLM) inference has two fundamentally different phases. Prefill is compute-bound. It processes the entire input prompt in parallel to generate the initial key-value (KV) cache. Decode is memory-bound. It generates one token at a time and requires substantial memory bandwidth to access model weights and the growing KV cache. By disaggregating these into specialized engines, you can assign different parallel strategies to each phase. With this separation, you can tune time to first token (TTFT) and inter-token latency (ITL) independently, control tail latency more reliably than chunked prefill tuning, and keep long-context prefills from blocking ongoing decode requests. vLLM improves single-node efficiency through continuous batching and PagedAttention. However, organizations that deploy at scale still face challenges when they orchestrate multi-node deployments and optimize routing.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this post, we show how to implement DPD with vLLM on &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/ai/hyperpod/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon SageMaker HyperPod&lt;/a&gt; using the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/sagemaker-hyperpod-model-deployment.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;HyperPod Inference Operator&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/07/ML-20985-1.jpg" alt="Disaggregated prefill and decode architecture running prefill and decode on separate GPU pools connected over EFA RDMA" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="when-to-use-disaggregated-inference"&gt;When to use disaggregated inference&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Disaggregating prefill and decode delivers the strongest gains for long-context, high-concurrency streaming workloads: chat assistants, agentic pipelines, document-analysis endpoints, and Retrieval Augmented Generation (RAG) with large retrieved contexts. In these cases, a single long prompt on a colocated GPU stalls in-flight decode for every other request, causing per-token latency spikes that DPD removes by construction.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Consider DPD when your workload has:&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Input prompts that regularly exceed 4,096 tokens.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Multiple concurrent users or requests.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Streaming responses where consistent token delivery matters.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Mixed traffic with both long and short prompts.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;A colocated deployment is the simpler choice when GPU contention is not a real concern: batch or offline workloads optimizing for TTFT, low-concurrency deployments, or short-prompt-only traffic. Below the routing threshold, the fixed cost of transferring KV cache over EFA RDMA outweighs the benefit of isolating decode. The DPD router sends those requests straight to a decoder. A single endpoint therefore handles mixed long and short traffic automatically, without manual routing logic.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;DPD requires at minimum one prefill node and one decode node with RDMA-capable EFA networking. For supported instance types, see the &lt;a href="#deploy-a-dpd-model-endpoint-to-your-hyperpod-cluster"&gt;Deploy a DPD model endpoint to your HyperPod cluster&lt;/a&gt; section.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="architecture"&gt;Architecture&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The HyperPod DPD implementation is built on the &lt;a href="https://vllm.ai/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;vLLM&lt;/a&gt; Production Stack router, with &lt;a href="https://lmcache.ai/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;LMCache&lt;/a&gt; providing the KV cache transfer layer over NIXL and EFA. The deployment has three components plus a transport stack.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/07/ML-20985-2.png" alt="HyperPod DPD architecture with an intelligent router directing requests to prefiller and decoder pods, and LMCache transferring KV cache over NIXL and EFA" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="intelligent-router"&gt;Intelligent router&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The router is the control plane. It tokenizes each prompt and applies a configurable token threshold to decide whether the request takes the disaggregated path or runs end-to-end on a decoder. Long-context prompts go through a prefiller then a decoder. Short prompts skip the prefiller, avoiding cross-GPU KV transfer that isn’t worthwhile. For disaggregated requests, it directs the prefiller to compute and push KV cache to a decoder, then forwards the request to that decoder for generation. It also supports per-prefiller routing strategies (&lt;code&gt;prefixaware&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;kvaware&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;session&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;roundrobin&lt;/code&gt;) through &lt;code&gt;intelligentRoutingSpec.routingStrategy&lt;/code&gt; to maximize cache locality across replicas.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="prefiller-pod"&gt;Prefiller pod&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The prefiller is a vLLM worker with LMCache as its KV connector through &lt;code&gt;LMCacheConnectorV1&lt;/code&gt;. It computes KV cache for long prompts and pushes it to the chosen decoder via LMCache’s PD sender backend layer-by-layer, overlapping compute and transfer to keep its GPUs saturated. LMCache also gives each prefiller an L1 CPU cache. When a prefix recurs (system prompts, multi-turn history, retrieval contexts), it serves from CPU memory without GPU recomputation. This produces significant TTFT gains. Activating DPD on an &lt;code&gt;InferenceEndpointConfig&lt;/code&gt; provisions both the connector and the cache automatically.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="decoder-pod"&gt;Decoder pod&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The decoder is a vLLM worker with LMCache as its receiver. It reserves GPU memory (the PD buffer, sized by &lt;code&gt;PD_BUFFER_SIZE&lt;/code&gt;) for incoming KV transfers. It runs full CUDA graphs for the decode kernel and starts generation as soon as the transfer completes. Because it never executes prefill, decode latency stays stable under concurrency and adding a long-context request never disturbs tokens already streaming.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="kv-transfer"&gt;KV transfer&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;KV cache transfer uses a four-layer stack (LMCache PD → NIXL → &lt;code&gt;libfabric&lt;/code&gt; → EFA) that HyperPod composes end-to-end. LMCache’s PD backend orchestrates the prefiller-side put and decoder-side retrieval. NIXL provides a unified memory abstraction across GPU, CPU, and remote peers and selects the right RDMA operation. The &lt;code&gt;libfabric&lt;/code&gt; provider exposes EFA as kernel-bypass, GPU-Direct RDMA, keeping the host CPU off the data path. This makes transfer cost negligible relative to prefill compute: on &lt;code&gt;ml.p5.48xlarge&lt;/code&gt; with 3,200 Gbps of EFA, an 8,000-token transfer for Llama 3.3 70B takes single-digit milliseconds. HyperPod ships the stack pre-integrated, so you select a DPD-supported worker image and the operator wires up the connector, NIXL, and EFA on every pod.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="deployment-overview"&gt;Deployment overview&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Disaggregated Prefill and Decode (DPD) is a functionality implemented by the HyperPod Inference Operator. This section covers prerequisites, installation of the inference operator and deployment of an inference endpoint that uses DPD for efficiently serving a Llama 70B model.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="prerequisites-and-hyperpod-inference-operator-installation"&gt;Prerequisites and HyperPod Inference Operator installation&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Make sure you have the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/cli/latest/userguide/cli-chap-getting-started.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Command Line Interface&lt;/a&gt; (AWS CLI), &lt;a href="https://kubernetes.io/docs/tasks/tools/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;kubectl&lt;/a&gt; access to your HyperPod cluster, a &lt;a href="https://huggingface.co/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;HuggingFace&lt;/a&gt; token, and sufficient service quota. Set up your local &lt;code&gt;kubectl&lt;/code&gt; configuration to connect to your HyperPod cluster. For more information, see &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/sagemaker-hyperpod-model-deployment-dpd.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Disaggregated Prefill and Decode for HyperPod inference&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;DPD requires HyperPod Inference Operator version 3.2 or later. The operator is installed by default on new HyperPod EKS clusters. For installation, setup, and upgrade instructions, see &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/architecture/unlock-efficient-model-deployment-simplified-inference-operator-setup-on-amazon-sagemaker-hyperpod/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Unlock efficient model deployment: simplified Inference Operator setup on Amazon SageMaker HyperPod&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Verify your operator version by running:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;kubectl get deployment hyperpod-inference-operator-controller-manager \
  -n hyperpod-inference-system \
  -o jsonpath='{.spec.template.spec.containers[?(@.name=="manager")].image}{"\n"}'&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The output is the full container image reference. The tag at the end encodes the version, for example:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;XXXXXXXXXXX.dkr.ecr.us-east-2.amazonaws.com/hyperpod-inference-operator:v3.2&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;If your operator version is not up-to-date, upgrade before continuing by following the upgrade instructions in the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/sagemaker-hyperpod-inference-release-notes.html#sagemaker-hyperpod-inference-release-notes-20260506" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;HyperPod Inference Operator Release Notes&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="deploy-a-dpd-model-endpoint-to-your-hyperpod-cluster"&gt;Deploy a DPD model endpoint to your HyperPod cluster&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;In this example, we deploy the Meta Llama 3.3 70B model on two &lt;code&gt;ml.p5.48xlarge&lt;/code&gt; instances. Verify that the instances are available in an instance group within the HyperPod cluster before proceeding. For DPD inference deployments, choose instance types that support both NVLink and EFA. EFA needs to support RDMA in read and write mode. This includes the &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/ec2/instance-types/p5/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;P5&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/ec2/instance-types/p6/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;P6&lt;/a&gt; instance families on AWS. Note that instances are required to be located within the same Availability Zone (AZ) for EFA high-bandwidth communication. Although G6, G6e, and G7e instance families do support EFA with RDMA read/write, performance on multi-GPU instances is bottlenecked by GPU-to-GPU communication over PCIe.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The worker image for the inference deployment must include vLLM, LMCache, NVIDIA NIXL, and the EFA &lt;code&gt;libfabric&lt;/code&gt; provider. At the time of writing, we support two image options:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Open source LMCache: &lt;a href="https://hub.docker.com/r/lmcache/vllm-openai/tags" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;lmcache/vllm-openai v0.4.3&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;SageMaker Deep Learning Container (DLC): vllm:server-hyperpod-cuda-v1.1.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="model-checkpoint-location"&gt;Model checkpoint location&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The HyperPod Inference Operator supports a broad range of checkpoint loading sources, including &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/sagemaker-hyperpod-model-deployment-deploy-ftm.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Simple Storage Service (Amazon S3) buckets, Amazon FSx file systems, and direct pulling from HuggingFace&lt;/a&gt; and the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/sagemaker-hyperpod-model-deployment-deploy-nvme.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;instance NVMe storage&lt;/a&gt;. For this post, we load the model checkpoint from an Amazon S3 bucket.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Verify that you have downloaded your preferred model checkpoint to an S3 bucket in the same Region as your HyperPod cluster. If you haven’t done so yet, set up your bucket name and HuggingFace token, then download Meta Llama 3.3 70B Instruct from HuggingFace and sync it to Amazon S3. To achieve high-bandwidth networking to Amazon S3, we recommend that you run this from an Amazon Elastic Compute Cloud (Amazon EC2) instance.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;export MODEL_BUCKET=&amp;lt;YOUR_BUCKET&amp;gt;
export MODEL_PREFIX=Llama-3.3-70B-Instruct
export AWS_REGION=&amp;lt;CLUSTER_REGION&amp;gt;
export HF_TOKEN=&amp;lt;YOUR_HUGGINGFACE_TOKEN&amp;gt;
pip install -U "huggingface_hub[cli]" "huggingface_hub[hf-transfer]"
HF_HUB_ENABLE_HF_TRANSFER=1 hf download meta-llama/Llama-3.3-70B-Instruct \
  --local-dir ./$MODEL_PREFIX \
  --token "$HF_TOKEN"
aws s3 sync ./$MODEL_PREFIX \
  s3://$MODEL_BUCKET/$MODEL_PREFIX/ \
  --region "$AWS_REGION"&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Prepare the model deployment manifest and change the environment variables as needed:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;export DEPLOYMENT_NAME="dpd-test-deployment"
export ENDPOINT_NAME="dpd-test"
export MODEL_NAME="meta-llama-3-3-70b"
export NAMESPACE="default"
export INSTANCE_TYPE="ml.p5.48xlarge"
export GPUS_PER_NODE="8"
export MODEL_IMAGE="lmcache/vllm-openai:v0.4.3"&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For the full deployment YAML, see &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/sagemaker-hyperpod-model-deployment-dpd.html#sagemaker-hyperpod-model-deployment-dpd-deploy" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Deploy a DPD endpoint&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h4 id="dpd-relevant-fields-in-the-deployment-manifest"&gt;DPD-relevant fields in the deployment manifest&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Most &lt;code&gt;InferenceEndpointConfig&lt;/code&gt; fields are shared with non-DPD endpoints and documented in the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/sagemaker-hyperpod-model-deployment.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Inference Operator documentation&lt;/a&gt;. The fields below are required or have different semantics for DPD.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;spec.pdSpec&lt;/strong&gt;: Declares the prefill/decode topology and specifies arguments. Presence of this field is what makes the endpoint disaggregated: the operator creates separate &lt;code&gt;Deployment&lt;/code&gt; objects for prefill and decode and wires them together through the router and LMCache PD backend.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;code&gt;replicas&lt;/code&gt;: Scale prefill and decode independently.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;code&gt;resources&lt;/code&gt;: Applied to the role’s pod spec. Top-level &lt;code&gt;worker.resources&lt;/code&gt; is ignored for DPD pods. Per-role values override.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;code&gt;routingThreshold&lt;/code&gt;: Token length threshold above which requests use the disaggregated path. Below the threshold, requests bypass the prefiller and go directly to the decoder.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;code&gt;args&lt;/code&gt;: vLLM flags specific to that role, merged into &lt;code&gt;worker.args&lt;/code&gt; at startup. Flags already in &lt;code&gt;worker.args&lt;/code&gt; are replaced with the per-role value, and flags not present are appended.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;spec.worker.environmentVariables&lt;/strong&gt;: These environment variables are applied identically to both the prefiller and decoder containers. There is no per-role environment-variable field today. For per-role behavior, use &lt;code&gt;pdSpec.{prefillSpec,decodingSpec}.args&lt;/code&gt; instead.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;More details about environment variables are in &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/sagemaker-hyperpod-model-deployment-dpd.html#sagemaker-hyperpod-model-deployment-dpd-deploy" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Deploy a DPD endpoint&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="apply-the-manifest-and-validate-the-deployment"&gt;Apply the manifest and validate the deployment&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;kubectl apply -f inference_endpoint_dpd_config.yaml&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The operator creates two &lt;code&gt;Deployment&lt;/code&gt; objects in your namespace and a router &lt;code&gt;Deployment&lt;/code&gt; in &lt;code&gt;hyperpod-inference-system&lt;/code&gt;. Image pull and model load take a few minutes. The pods first enter &lt;code&gt;ContainerCreating&lt;/code&gt;, then become &lt;code&gt;Running&lt;/code&gt; as containers come up. List the pods across both namespaces:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;kubectl get pods -A \
  | grep -E "prefill-${DEPLOYMENT_NAME}|decode-${DEPLOYMENT_NAME}|${DEPLOYMENT_NAME}-${NAMESPACE}-router"&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;NAMESPACE                   NAME                                           READY   STATUS    RESTARTS   AGE
default                     prefill-dpd-test-deployment-XXXX               3/3     Running   0          7m
default                     decode-dpd-test-deployment-XXXX                3/3     Running   0          7m
hyperpod-inference-system   dpd-test-deployment-default-router-XXXX        2/2     Running   0          7m&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Each model pod has 3 containers: the vLLM worker, an Nginx reverse proxy, and an OpenTelemetry collector. The router pod has 2 containers (router, otel). The IEC condition reports readiness:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;kubectl get inferenceendpointconfig ${DEPLOYMENT_NAME} -n ${NAMESPACE} \
  -o jsonpath='{.status.conditions[0].message}{"\n"}'&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;DPD prefill and decode deployments are ready&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="invoke-the-endpoint-and-verify-kv-transfer"&gt;Invoke the endpoint and verify KV transfer&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Once the endpoint is ready, send a short and a long prompt to exercise both routing paths. Check the prefiller and decoder logs to confirm KV cache is being transferred over EFA. The following commands assume the IEC was deployed with &lt;code&gt;${NAMESPACE}&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;${ENDPOINT_NAME}&lt;/code&gt;, and &lt;code&gt;${DEPLOYMENT_NAME}&lt;/code&gt; set as in the preceding manifest.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Get the required pod names and router url:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;PREFILL_POD=$(kubectl get pod -n ${NAMESPACE} \
  -l 'inference.sagemaker.aws.amazon.com/dpd-role=prefill' \
  -o jsonpath='{.items[0].metadata.name}')
DECODE_POD=$(kubectl get pod -n ${NAMESPACE} \
  -l 'inference.sagemaker.aws.amazon.com/dpd-role=decode' \
  -o jsonpath='{.items[0].metadata.name}')
ROUTER_POD=$(kubectl get pods -n hyperpod-inference-system -o name \
  | grep -- "${DEPLOYMENT_NAME}-${NAMESPACE}-router" | head -1)
ROUTER_URL=http://${DEPLOYMENT_NAME}-${NAMESPACE}-routing-service.hyperpod-inference-system.svc.cluster.local:443/v1/chat/completions&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="short-prompt-below-threshold-direct-to-decoder-path"&gt;Short prompt (below threshold, direct-to-decoder path)&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Run a short prompt through a pod within the cluster that invokes the endpoint:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;kubectl run curl-short --rm -it --image=curlimages/curl --restart=Never -- \
  curl -s -k -X POST "$ROUTER_URL" \
    -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
    -d '{
      "model": "/opt/ml/model",
      "messages": [{"role": "user", "content": "What is disaggregated prefill-decode in one sentence?"}],
      "max_tokens": 80,
      "temperature": 0.0
    }'&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-json"&gt;{
  "id": "chatcmpl-7-...",
  "object": "chat.completion",
  "model": "/opt/ml/model",
  "choices": [{
    "index": 0,
    "message": {"role": "assistant", "content": "Disaggregated prefill-decode is an inference architecture that ..."},
    "finish_reason": "stop"
  }]
}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="long-prompt-above-threshold-dpd-path"&gt;Long prompt (above threshold, DPD path)&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;A prompt above the 4,096-token routing threshold is routed through the prefiller, then to the decoder for token generation. The following example builds a roughly 6,000-token prompt by repeating a sentence:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;kubectl run curl-long --rm -it --image=curlimages/curl --restart=Never -- sh -c '
ROUTER="'"$ROUTER_URL"'"
LONG=""
i=0; while [ $i -lt 600 ]; do LONG="${LONG}The quick brown fox jumps over the lazy dog. "; i=$((i+1)); done
curl -s -k -X POST "$ROUTER" \
  -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
  -d "{\"model\":\"/opt/ml/model\",\"messages\":[{\"role\":\"user\",\"content\":\"${LONG}\"}],\"max_tokens\":30,\"temperature\":0.0}"
'&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-json"&gt;{
  "id": "chatcmpl-7-...",
  "object": "chat.completion",
  "model": "/opt/ml/model",
  "choices": [{
    "index": 0,
    "message": {"role": "assistant", "content": "The text is a repetitive sequence of ..."},
    "finish_reason": "length"
  }]
}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To confirm that the invocation used the disaggregated path, we can check the router pod logs:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;kubectl logs $ROUTER_POD -n hyperpod-inference-system -c router-container --tail=20 \
  | grep -E "Conditional routing|prefill selection|prefill time|to decoder"&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For the long prompt, you can observe the following output:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-plaintext"&gt;[INFO] Conditional routing: estimated_tokens=6750, threshold=4096, disaggregate=True
[INFO] DPD prefill selection: delegating to PrefixAwareRouter with 1 endpoints
[INFO] DPD prefill selection: PrefixAwareRouter selected http://10.1.54.203:8081
[INFO] &amp;lt;req-id&amp;gt; prefill time (TTFT): 7.1913
[INFO] Routing request &amp;lt;req-id&amp;gt; to http://10.1.54.203:8081 at 1780486382.5954, process time = 7.1929
[INFO] Routing request &amp;lt;req-id&amp;gt; to decoder http://10.1.172.158:8081 at 1780486382.6098, process time = 0.0144&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;code&gt;disaggregate=True&lt;/code&gt; confirms the request took the prefiller path. The two &lt;code&gt;Routing request&lt;/code&gt; lines show the prefill hop followed by the decode hop.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="scaling-guidance"&gt;Scaling guidance&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;DPD currently supports a single decoder replica with multiple prefiller replicas. This means you scale prefill capacity independently while the decoder remains fixed at one instance.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Start with a 1:1 prefill-to-decode ratio&lt;/strong&gt; for balanced workloads (chat, code generation) where input and output lengths are comparable. &lt;strong&gt;Scale to 2:1 or 3:1&lt;/strong&gt; when your workload is prefill-heavy: summarization, classification, or RAG with long retrieved contexts. This ratio is appropriate when you observe TTFT climbing under load while per-token output latency (TPOT) remains stable.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;With multiple prefillers, set &lt;code&gt;intelligentRoutingSpec.routingStrategy&lt;/code&gt; on your workload. Use &lt;code&gt;kvaware&lt;/code&gt; for workloads with repeated prefixes (this maximizes L1 cache hits across prefiller partitions). Use &lt;code&gt;session&lt;/code&gt; for multi-turn conversations that benefit from keeping a user’s context on one prefiller.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;If instead TPOT is climbing and output throughput plateaus despite prefiller availability, the single decoder is saturated. In that case, increase &lt;code&gt;PD_BUFFER_SIZE&lt;/code&gt;, reduce &lt;code&gt;max-model-len&lt;/code&gt;, or reduce concurrency to the endpoint until multi-decoder support is available.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="benchmarking-performance"&gt;Benchmarking performance&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Benchmarks used &lt;code&gt;genai-bench&lt;/code&gt; with fixed-length synthetic prompts (4,096 input tokens, 256 output tokens) at concurrency levels 8, 16, and 32. Each concurrency level ran until results stabilized. DPD configuration: 1 prefiller and 1 decoder across 2 nodes (16 GPUs), KV-aware routing, &lt;code&gt;enforce-eager&lt;/code&gt; on the prefiller, and CUDA graphs on the decoder. Baseline: single node (8 GPUs), same model and GPU settings. Hardware: &lt;code&gt;ml.p5.48xlarge&lt;/code&gt; (8x H100 80GB, EFA enabled). Model: Llama-3.3-70B-Instruct with &lt;code&gt;tensor-parallel-size=8&lt;/code&gt; and &lt;code&gt;max-model-len=16,384&lt;/code&gt;. The following charts show the percentage improvement DPD delivers over the colocated baseline on two instance families. Higher bars indicate larger DPD advantage.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/07/ML-20985-3.jpg" alt="Bar chart of DPD percentage improvement over the colocated baseline on ml.p5.48xlarge H100 instances across concurrency levels" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following chart shows the same benchmarks on &lt;code&gt;ml.p5en.48xlarge&lt;/code&gt; instances with H200 GPUs, where DPD gains are equally pronounced.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/07/ML-20985-4.jpg" alt="Bar chart of DPD percentage improvement over the colocated baseline on ml.p5e.48xlarge H200 instances across concurrency levels" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Across both hardware configurations, DPD delivers consistent gains on per-token output latency (TPOT), end-to-end latency, and throughput as concurrency grows:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Per-token latency stays flat under load&lt;/strong&gt;. DPD isolates decode from prefill interference, keeping TPOT constant regardless of concurrent long-context requests. For D(4096,256) workloads at concurrency 8 to 32, improvement ranges from 22% at low concurrency to 66% at high concurrency on H100, and 28% to 48% on H200.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Throughput scales with concurrency&lt;/strong&gt;. The dedicated decoder runs at full CUDA graph efficiency without prefill interruption. Output throughput improves up to 35% on H100 and up to 64% on H200 at higher concurrency.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;End-to-end latency improves at P50&lt;/strong&gt;. The cumulative TPOT savings across output tokens outweigh the KV transfer cost. E2E P50 improves 14-32% on H100 and 29-41% on H200.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;DPD does introduce a modest increase in time to first token because of the KV cache transfer over EFA RDMA. For streaming workloads where consistent per-token delivery matters more than initial response, this tradeoff is favorable. The conditional routing threshold (default 4,096 tokens) is designed to make sure that short requests bypass disaggregation entirely, avoiding transfer overhead where it is not needed.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="observability"&gt;Observability&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You can monitor DPD metrics through the SageMaker HyperPod Observability features. For more information, see &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/accelerate-foundation-model-development-with-one-click-observability-in-amazon-sagemaker-hyperpod/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Accelerate foundation model development with one-click observability in Amazon SageMaker HyperPod&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;DPD metrics are available in the Inference dashboard.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/07/ML-20985-5.jpg" alt="DPD metrics shown in the SageMaker HyperPod Inference dashboard" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Additional metrics that may be useful are CPU/GPU usage, which is available in the &lt;strong&gt;Tasks&lt;/strong&gt; dashboard, as well as the metrics available in the &lt;strong&gt;Cluster Overview&lt;/strong&gt; dashboard.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="clean-up"&gt;Clean up&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To avoid ongoing charges, delete the resources created during this walkthrough when you are done experimenting.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Delete the &lt;code&gt;InferenceEndpointConfig&lt;/code&gt; to remove the prefill pod, decode pod, router, and all associated services: 
  &lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
   &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;kubectl delete inferenceendpointconfig ${DEPLOYMENT_NAME} -n ${NAMESPACE}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
  &lt;/div&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;(Optional) Remove the model from S3 if you uploaded it specifically for this walkthrough: 
  &lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
   &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;aws s3 rm s3://${MODEL_BUCKET}/${MODEL_PREFIX}/ --recursive --region ${AWS_REGION}&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
  &lt;/div&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;(Optional) Scale down or remove the HyperPod instance group if the GPU instances were provisioned solely for this deployment. Refer to the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/smcluster-scale-down.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Managing HyperPod clusters&lt;/a&gt; documentation for instructions.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Disaggregated Prefill and Decode (DPD) on Amazon SageMaker HyperPod runs prefill and decode on separate GPU pools. KV cache transfers between them over EFA using GPU-Direct RDMA. Prefill is compute-bound. Decode is memory-bandwidth-bound. When colocated, the two phases compete for the same GPU resources. A single long prompt can stall in-flight decoding and inflate tail per-token latency. Separating them removes that interference, produces more predictable latency under mixed traffic, and lets you scale each phase independently.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The HyperPod Inference Operator handles the underlying orchestration: provisioning the router, wiring the prefill and decode pods together, and integrating with HyperPod observability. You activate DPD by adding a few fields to the same &lt;code&gt;InferenceEndpointConfig&lt;/code&gt; resource you already use for non-disaggregated endpoints.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You can get started today by deploying a DPD endpoint on your HyperPod EKS cluster following the steps in this post. To learn more, visit the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/sagemaker-hyperpod.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon SageMaker HyperPod documentation&lt;/a&gt;, the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sagemaker/latest/dg/sagemaker-hyperpod-model-deployment.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;HyperPod Inference Operator model deployment guide&lt;/a&gt; or directly try out the example manifest in this post.&lt;/p&gt;

			</item>
		<item>
		<title>MCP tool design: Practical approaches and tradeoffs</title>
		<link>https://aws.amazon.com/blogs/machine-learning/mcp-tool-design-practical-approaches-and-tradeoffs/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Daniel Wells]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Thu, 09 Jul 2026 16:40:16 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Advanced (300)]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Artificial Intelligence]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Best Practices]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">ec453ef7a91a910b45a38291318bb152653d0e4a</guid>

					<description>In this post, we show where MCP tool design goes wrong and how to fix it with practical context engineering approaches.</description>
										<content:encoded>&lt;p&gt;When &lt;a href="https://modelcontextprotocol.io/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Model Context Protocol (MCP)&lt;/a&gt; tools underperform, the cause is rarely the protocol itself but the tool design. Many teams start by exposing an existing API as-is and trusting the agent to figure out the rest. It is a natural way to extend APIs to agentic systems and generative AI coding tools. For straightforward use cases, it can work. But often it does not.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You must design your tools for how large language models (LLMs) and agentic systems work. Without this, you risk failed tool calls, wrong parameter values, and retries that waste context and degrade performance. In this post, we show where MCP tool design goes wrong and how to fix it with practical &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/prescriptive-guidance/latest/gen-ai-lifecycle-operational-excellence/dev-experimenting-context.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;context engineering&lt;/a&gt; approaches.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Two problems are behind most of these failures. The first is &lt;em&gt;bloat&lt;/em&gt;. Tool definitions load into the LLM’s context on every call, whether the tool is used or not. Multiple connected MCP servers can consume significant context before the user has asked a single question. As context fills, an LLM’s &lt;a href="https://research.trychroma.com/context-rot" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;ability to reason can degrade&lt;/a&gt; and cause the session to become less productive.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The second is &lt;em&gt;confusion&lt;/em&gt;. As reasoning degrades, the LLM makes poorer choices, calls the wrong tool and chooses incorrect parameters. Subsequent retries compound the issue by further contributing to bloat. Semantic similarity between tools, too many options, and ambiguous naming &lt;a href="https://arxiv.org/abs/2601.20412" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;also contribute to confusion&lt;/a&gt;. A common solution is to enrich the tool descriptions with clearer definitions, natural language mappings, and usage examples. This does help with confusion. But everything added risks worsening bloat and compounding the very issue you are working to solve.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This pattern is common. Your agent makes unexpected choices, the context fills sooner than expected, and your session quickly becomes less useful. Addressing bloat and confusion is a context engineering problem. Context engineering shapes what the LLM sees and when it sees it, so the model produces better results. Improving one, or both, of these is a complex balancing act.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following sections explore approaches and tradeoffs to address these issues. To make them concrete, we built several examples that expose a simulated K-12 content search API using the MCP protocol. You will run them locally and interact with them using &lt;a href="https://kiro.dev/cli/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Kiro CLI&lt;/a&gt; to compare the differences yourself.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="approaches-and-tradeoffs"&gt;Approaches and tradeoffs&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The approaches we share address these issues at the tool level. Some shape what the LLM sees or when it sees it. Others change how tools are structured. As you apply them, you need to understand how they impact bloat and confusion to find the right balance between the two.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="descriptions-and-responses"&gt;Descriptions and responses&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The natural first step to fixing MCP tool behavior is to improve the descriptions. A reliable way to reduce confusion is to clarify what values mean, how natural language maps to them, and what the tool is for. But take it too far, and you quickly add to bloat. Context and cost can grow quickly when many MCP servers are loaded into a client.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;What the tool returns shapes behavior too. A tool that returns 50 fields per result fills context quickly. If 5 are sufficient for a decision, default the response to those fields and provide a separate option to request a detailed view. Shifting to an on-demand approach for detailed output cuts response tokens by roughly two-thirds, &lt;a href="https://www.anthropic.com/engineering/writing-tools-for-agents" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;according to Anthropic’s research&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Proper error messages are another way to improve efficiency. When a tool call fails, helpful errors can steer the next attempt. A response that says “search requires 2 or more terms in query” tells the LLM exactly what to change. A response that returns only “no results” gives it nothing, causing the model to either give up or keep guessing.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="schema-constraints"&gt;Schema constraints&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Where descriptions and proper errors guide the LLM toward correct values, schema constraints like enums and default values can remove the guesswork entirely. Consider the following to address common schema issues:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Rename parameters to match how the LLM might understand the domain, not how your database labels its columns. 
  &lt;ul&gt; 
   &lt;li&gt;A parameter called &lt;code&gt;resource_class&lt;/code&gt; with values like ‘Student Resource’ or ‘Teacher Support’ is clearer for the LLM. A &lt;code&gt;content_bucket&lt;/code&gt; column requires the LLM to know an internal convention.&lt;/li&gt; 
  &lt;/ul&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Set defaults to the most common values so the LLM only needs to specify what varies.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Constrain finite-value fields with enums so the schema itself tells the LLM what is valid.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Drop fields that are rarely used or that the LLM cannot use well. 
  &lt;ul&gt; 
   &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/prescriptive-guidance/latest/mcp-strategies/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS Prescriptive Guidance for MCP Strategies&lt;/a&gt; recommends keeping tool parameter counts around eight or fewer.&lt;/li&gt; 
  &lt;/ul&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="restructuring-tools-and-on-demand-context"&gt;Restructuring tools and on-demand context&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Splitting a multi-purpose tool into several specific tools provides clarity to the model and gives more granular results. A lazy loading discovery tool is one example. You can remove complex tool descriptions from the always-loaded context and provide a separate tool for on-demand retrieval. The LLM only retrieves this context when the task requires it. This keeps context lean and focused. Anthropic’s Tool Search Tool and &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock-agentcore/latest/devguide/gateway.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock AgentCore Gateway&lt;/a&gt; show these concepts applied at scale. Anthropic reports &lt;a href="https://www.anthropic.com/engineering/advanced-tool-use" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;up to 85% token reduction&lt;/a&gt; by loading tool definitions only when relevant.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/agent-toolkit/latest/userguide/skills.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Skills&lt;/a&gt; are another example of lazy loading, but implemented client side. These local files contain useful context for the tool but are only read into context when relevant. This reduces the effort to implement and distribute them. But there is no guarantee that the skill loads when needed or remains unchanged once installed.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="server-side-inference"&gt;Server-side inference&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Context engineering is challenging when you do not control which model will interpret your instructions. And once deployed, you may not control which LLM calls your MCP tools. A description tuned against one model might confuse another. Fully testing all the potential models a client might use is unrealistic.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;One way to address this is to add an introspection tool that directly calls an external LLM. The client uses natural language to query your introspection tool and gets targeted instructions for using the other tools. Because you choose the model, you can properly prompt engineer this tool and test with golden queries. The client LLM still makes the final tool call, but your LLM interprets the need and provides correct values. Because the use case is focused, a smaller, faster model handles this task well and keeps costs reasonable at scale.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="agentic-tools"&gt;Agentic tools&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;When you need accuracy and full control, the next logical progression is to back your entire MCP server with an agent of your own. The introspection example handles a single interpretation step, but an agentic tool handles the entire interaction. The tools become natural language endpoints to the client. It states what it needs, and your agent does the rest. The other techniques covered still apply. The tradeoffs are yours to own completely, but as with introspection, you can fully engineer the behavior with the model of your choice.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The next section walks through working code that applies these approaches against the same backend and test queries, so you can compare their behavior directly.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="walkthrough"&gt;Walkthrough&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Each of the 6 versions wraps the same simulated K-12 content search backend with a different MCP tool design. The backend has 14 filterable fields (subject, grade, format, standards alignment, language, resource class, and others) with controlled vocabularies. The challenge for the LLM is bridging the gap between how a teacher phrases a request and the exact values those fields accept. V1 starts with the raw passthrough that leaves this gap open. Each later version closes it differently.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="prerequisites"&gt;Prerequisites&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;You need the following to run the sample code:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Python 3.10+.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;SQLite (included with Python).&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://kiro.dev/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Kiro&lt;/a&gt; (free tier available) or another MCP client.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;AWS account with Amazon Bedrock model access (V5 and V6 only): &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/model-card-amazon-nova-2-lite.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Nova 2 Lite&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/model-card-anthropic-claude-sonnet-4-6.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Anthropic’s Claude Sonnet 4.6&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Clone the &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-mcp-tool-design-patterns" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;sample code repository&lt;/a&gt; and follow the repository README for detailed prerequisites, installation, and server startup instructions. The walkthrough that follows assumes all six versions are running.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;No infrastructure is deployed to AWS. You run all code locally. Inference calls to Amazon Bedrock are the only cost aside from your coding client. See &lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/bedrock/pricing/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock pricing&lt;/a&gt; for current rates.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This walkthrough assumes you are using Kiro and have run &lt;code&gt;python scripts/setup_agents.py&lt;/code&gt; from the repository.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="test-queries"&gt;Test queries&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;These queries return real results from the sample database. Try them against each version:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ol type="1"&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;“Find me a quiz on fractions for my 7th graders.”&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;“Do you have any lessons for teaching Spanish in middle school?”&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;“I need TEKS-aligned content for kids working on dividing in middle school.”&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;“What types of content can I search for?”&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;“Can I get details on n-sc-1096?”&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ol&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;As you run through each version:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Observe whether the LLM picked the right filter values on the first call.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Note how many tool calls it took.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Check whether the response included information the LLM didn’t need.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Observe how quickly the context window fills. Check the percentage displayed in Kiro, or use &lt;code&gt;/context show&lt;/code&gt; for a detailed breakdown.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/26/ML-20889-1.png" alt="Kiro CLI showing fractions assessment search results from the v2-descriptions agent, with the /agent swap v3-schema command being entered" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To switch between versions, use &lt;code&gt;/agent swap&lt;/code&gt; (for example, &lt;code&gt;/agent swap v1-passthrough&lt;/code&gt;). Clear context with &lt;code&gt;/clear&lt;/code&gt; between versions so prior results don’t influence the next one.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="v1-raw-passthrough"&gt;V1: Raw passthrough&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;code&gt;v1_passthrough.py&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The first version is the baseline anti-pattern. It exposes the backend API directly. The tool definition has 14 parameters with internal names like &lt;code&gt;discipline&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;media_type&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;content_bucket&lt;/code&gt;, and a one-line docstring that says “Performs a global search for educational resources.” No valid values listed, no natural language mappings, no guidance.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The tool definition is small, but the LLM has no guidance on what values are valid. Try the first query. It might pass “quiz” for &lt;code&gt;media_type&lt;/code&gt; when the valid value is “Assessment”, or “math” when the field expects “Math”. Each wrong choice triggers a retry that consumes more context. The only clue that something went wrong is an empty result. The LLM is left guessing what to change on the next attempt. Low baseline cost is misleading when confusion drives up the actual cost through churn.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/26/ML-20889-2.png" alt="Kiro context view for the v1-passthrough agent showing 4% context used, broken down by agent files, tools, Kiro responses, and prompts" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="v2-rich-descriptions"&gt;V2: Rich descriptions&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;code&gt;v2_better_descriptions.py&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Same structure as V1, zero backend refactoring. The docstring now lists valid values and synonym mappings for each field. For example, &lt;code&gt;discipline&lt;/code&gt; shows “Valid: Math, Science, Literacy/ELA…” and &lt;code&gt;media_type&lt;/code&gt; maps “‘quiz’/‘test’ → Assessment, ‘worksheet’ → Activity.” It also distinguishes fuzzy search (&lt;code&gt;keyword&lt;/code&gt;) from strict filters (everything else). Three rarely-used parameters are dropped and error messages now return guidance on which filters to add, rather than an empty result.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Try the same queries. Accuracy improves immediately because the LLM sees valid values and synonym mappings. The tool definition is noticeably larger. That’s the bloat tradeoff. Every call pays it whether the tool is used or not. After five queries, compare total context consumed to V1. The per-call overhead is higher, but fewer retries often make the total lower.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/26/ML-20889-3.png" alt="Kiro context view for the v2-descriptions agent showing 3% context used, with tools consuming a larger share than in V1" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="v3-schema-and-defaults"&gt;V3: Schema and defaults&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;code&gt;v3_rethought_schema.py&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This version renames parameters to match how the LLM thinks and constrains values through the schema itself. Parameters are renamed: &lt;code&gt;discipline&lt;/code&gt; becomes &lt;code&gt;subject&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;content_bucket&lt;/code&gt; becomes &lt;code&gt;resource_class&lt;/code&gt;. Each finite-value field uses a &lt;code&gt;Literal&lt;/code&gt; type that lists valid options directly in the schema. Sensible defaults handle the common case: &lt;code&gt;structure='Asset'&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;resource_class='Student Resource'&lt;/code&gt;, &lt;code&gt;language='en'&lt;/code&gt;. A separate &lt;code&gt;get_resource_detail&lt;/code&gt; tool handles drill-down. This applies the restructuring approach, giving each tool a clear job and keeping search responses concise.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Enums help prevent wrong values at the protocol level. Defaults mean the LLM only specifies what varies. The response includes a &lt;code&gt;defaults_applied&lt;/code&gt; field so the LLM knows what was filtered implicitly. The definition is smaller than V2 because names and enums do the work that verbose descriptions did before. Run the queries and compare accuracy to V2. Accuracy improves while context drops.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="v4-lazy-loading-restructuring"&gt;V4: Lazy loading (restructuring)&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;code&gt;v4_lazy_loading.py&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Instead of embedding enums and detailed descriptions in the search tool, this approach moves them behind a separate tool. The search tool keeps only short hint descriptions like “Subject area, e.g.&amp;nbsp;‘Math’, ‘Science’, ‘Literacy’”. A &lt;code&gt;get_taxonomy&lt;/code&gt; tool takes a list of field names and returns valid values and natural language mappings only for the fields relevant to the current query.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The search tool definition is the leanest so far. Notice the tool calls. For ambiguous queries, the LLM calls &lt;code&gt;get_taxonomy&lt;/code&gt; before searching to confirm valid values. For straightforward queries where the hints are sufficient, it might skip the taxonomy call entirely and search directly. Common values in the hints handle frequent queries without a round-trip, whereas the full taxonomy is available for edge cases. Because the taxonomy loads only when needed, every prior interaction in the session runs without that context cost. The savings in this example is modest, but in environments with many connected tools and complex tool schemas it compounds quickly.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/06/26/ML-20889-4.png" alt="Kiro context view for the v4-lazy agent showing 2% context used, the leanest baseline of the versions" width="600"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="v5-llm-introspection"&gt;V5: LLM introspection&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;code&gt;v5_llm_introspect.py&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;This version adds an introspect tool backed by Amazon Nova 2 Lite on Amazon Bedrock. The &lt;code&gt;introspect_query&lt;/code&gt; tool takes the teacher’s natural-language question and returns recommended filter values with rationale explaining each choice. It interprets “TEKS-aligned content for kids working on dividing in middle school” and returns recommended filters: &lt;code&gt;subject&lt;/code&gt; “Math”, grades 6-8, &lt;code&gt;state_standard&lt;/code&gt; “TX-TEKS”, &lt;code&gt;topic&lt;/code&gt; “dividing,division”.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Kiro still makes the final search call. Because introspect runs on a model of your choice, &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock/latest/userguide/prompt-engineering-guidelines.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;prompt engineering&lt;/a&gt; and testing are reliable. The interpretation happens server-side, so your context stays lean. The tradeoff is cost. You pay for the server call but results stay consistent regardless of which model Kiro uses. Switch models with &lt;code&gt;/model&lt;/code&gt; and compare V4 versus V5. Without introspection, weaker models produce inconsistent results. With introspection, results stay stable.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="v6-agent-as-tool"&gt;V6: Agent-as-tool&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;code&gt;v6/app/v6/main.py&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The final version exposes a single MCP tool backed by a &lt;a href="https://strandsagents.com/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Strands Agents&lt;/a&gt; agent with its own system prompt and internal tools. The external interface is one tool with one parameter: &lt;code&gt;agentic_search_content(question: str)&lt;/code&gt;. Your agent handles taxonomy lookup, search, detail retrieval, and response formatting internally using its own tools that the client LLM does not see.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Try all 5 queries and compare client context usage to the other versions. The client LLM does minimal work. Behavior is consistent regardless of which client connects because your agent owns the reasoning. Conversation history persists across calls, so follow-up questions work naturally. The tradeoff is cost and latency in exchange for direct control over behavior and consistency. Switch between models with &lt;code&gt;/model&lt;/code&gt; in Kiro and notice results remain stable across models.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="tradeoffs-at-a-glance"&gt;Tradeoffs at a glance&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Each version trades one cost for another. This table lays them side by side.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;table border="1px" width="100%" cellpadding="10px"&gt; 
 &lt;tbody&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Version&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Approach&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Tradeoff&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;V2&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Rich descriptions&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Accuracy up, definition larger&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;V3&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Schema + defaults&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Accuracy up, definition smaller&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;V4&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Restructuring + lazy loading&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Leanest baseline, extra round-trip&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;V5&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Server-side introspection&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Handles ambiguity, you pay for inference&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
  &lt;tr&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;V6&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Agent-as-tool&lt;/td&gt; 
   &lt;td&gt;Direct control, highest infrastructure cost&lt;/td&gt; 
  &lt;/tr&gt; 
 &lt;/tbody&gt; 
&lt;/table&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;After running the test queries across all 6 versions, notice the patterns. V2 is the fastest path to better results with no refactoring. V4 has the leanest baseline context. V5 handles ambiguous queries that other versions miss. V6 gives you the most control at the highest infrastructure cost. No version wins across all dimensions. The right choice depends on your field count, vocabulary stability, latency budget, and how much you need consistent behavior across different clients.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="cleaning-up"&gt;Cleaning up&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;All servers run locally. To stop them, run &lt;code&gt;scripts/stop_all.sh&lt;/code&gt; from the repository root. See the &lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-mcp-tool-design-patterns" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;accompanying repository README&lt;/a&gt; for details.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;MCP itself is not the problem. It is a key protocol for agentic solutions. The problem is tool design. The approaches in this post address bloat and confusion through context engineering at the tool level. The code walkthrough demonstrates each approach against the same scenario so you can observe the tradeoffs directly and decide what fits your situation.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The protocol continues to evolve. The &lt;a href="https://blog.modelcontextprotocol.io/posts/2026-mcp-roadmap/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;MCP 2026 Roadmap&lt;/a&gt; addresses transport scaling, agent communication, and features for enterprise deployment.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To go deeper, explore the following resources:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/prescriptive-guidance/latest/mcp-strategies/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;MCP Strategies on AWS&lt;/a&gt; – Covers the broader architecture: which MCP patterns to use, when to connect multiple servers, and how to structure your MCP system beyond a single tool.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/about-aws/whats-new/2026/05/aws-mcp-server/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;AWS MCP Server&lt;/a&gt; – A managed MCP server that gives AI coding agents access to AWS APIs, documentation search, and curated agent skills through a single connection. Study how it uses on-demand skill loading to keep context lean.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://strandsagents.com/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Strands Agents SDK&lt;/a&gt; – Go deeper on the agentic approach from V6. Build multi-turn agents with tool orchestration, memory, and testable behavior.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/bedrock-agentcore/latest/devguide/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock AgentCore&lt;/a&gt; – Deploy your MCP server without managing infrastructure. Includes runtime hosting, gateway for multi-server tool discovery, and persistent memory across sessions.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;For more MCP code examples and patterns:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://awslabs.github.io/mcp/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Open Source MCP Servers for AWS&lt;/a&gt; – 56 open source MCP servers covering documentation, infrastructure, AI/ML, data, developer tools, and more. Pick one and evaluate its tool descriptions against the bloat and confusion framework from this post.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://github.com/awslabs/agentcore-samples" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Bedrock AgentCore Samples&lt;/a&gt; – Getting-started guides, feature demos, use-case examples, blueprints, and workshops for AgentCore.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://github.com/aws-samples/sample-serverless-mcp-servers" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Sample Serverless MCP Servers&lt;/a&gt; – Reference implementations for hosting MCP servers on AWS Lambda and Amazon Elastic Container Service (Amazon ECS), including stateless and stateful patterns, plus Strands agents on Lambda.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="https://aws.amazon.com/solutions/guidance/vibe-coding-with-aws-mcp-servers/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Guidance for Vibe Coding with AWS MCP Servers&lt;/a&gt; – A sample hotel booking application demonstrating how AI coding assistants use AWS MCP servers to accelerate development with AgentCore.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt;
