&lt;h4 id="required-parameters"&gt;Required parameters&lt;/h4&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;ACCOUNT_ID = &amp;lt;&amp;lt;0358134xxxxx&amp;gt;&amp;gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;REGION = &amp;lt;&amp;gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;DATASET_ID = &amp;lt;&amp;lt;30c1e26a-e02a-4f2a-ac48-xxa1xxxxx916&amp;gt;&amp;gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;TOPIC_ID = &amp;lt;&amp;gt;&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="step-1-get-target-dataset-id"&gt;Step 1: Get target dataset ID&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Open the Quick Sight console. In the left navigation panel, select &lt;strong&gt;Data&lt;/strong&gt;, then switch to the &lt;strong&gt;Datasets&lt;/strong&gt; tab. Select the dataset that you want to enrich. The dataset ID is the UUID at the end of your browser’s URL.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Example:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/06/ML-21217-3.jpg" alt="Amazon Quick console dataset URL with the dataset ID at the end" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="step-2-get-the-source-legacy-topic-id"&gt;Step 2: Get the source legacy Topic ID&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Open the Quick Sight console. In the left navigation panel, select &lt;strong&gt;Data&lt;/strong&gt;, then switch to the &lt;strong&gt;Topics&lt;/strong&gt; tab. Select the source legacy Topic that you want to migrate from. The Topic ID is the string at the end of your browser’s URL.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Example:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/06/ML-21217-4.jpg" alt="Amazon Quick console Topics URL with the legacy Topic ID at the end" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h2 id="step-3-python-code-to-enrich-a-dataset-with-information-from-a-legacy-topic"&gt;Step 3: Python code to enrich a dataset with information from a legacy Topic&lt;/h2&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;The following script connects to the Quick Sight APIs, retrieves your existing legacy Topic metadata and target dataset schema, then intelligently maps every piece of business context to its new home in the dataset’s &lt;code&gt;SemanticModelConfiguration&lt;/code&gt;. Column descriptions and synonyms become &lt;code&gt;ColumnProperties&lt;/code&gt;, calculated fields are injected as &lt;code&gt;CreateColumnsStep&lt;/code&gt; entries in your data prep pipeline, and named entities, filters, and custom instructions are consolidated into a structured &lt;code&gt;CustomInstructions&lt;/code&gt; text block. After the mapping is complete, it applies the enriched payload directly to your dataset through a SigV4-signed API call. Your dataset becomes fully self-describing in a single execution, with no manual UI work and no risk of transcription errors.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Save the following script as &lt;code&gt;enrich_dataset.py&lt;/code&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-python"&gt;"""
enrich_dataset_local.py --- Enrich a Quick Sight dataset with Legacy Topic metadata.
Usage: python enrich_dataset_local.py &amp;lt;topic_id&amp;gt; &amp;lt;dataset_id&amp;gt; &amp;lt;aws_account_id&amp;gt; &amp;lt;aws_region&amp;gt;
NOTE: Uses raw SigV4 API call to bypass botocore validation (requires botocore &amp;gt;= 1.43.11).
"""
import json
import sys
import boto3
import botocore.session
from botocore.auth import SigV4Auth
from botocore.awsrequest import AWSRequest
import urllib3
http = urllib3.PoolManager()

def raw_update_data_set(aws_account_id, dataset_id, payload, region):
    """Call UpdateDataSet via raw SigV4 request, bypassing botocore validation."""
    session = botocore.session.get_session()
    credentials = session.get_credentials().get_frozen_credentials()
    url = f"https://quicksight.{region}.amazonaws.com/accounts/{aws_account_id}/data-sets/{dataset_id}"
    body = json.dumps(payload)
    headers = {"Content-Type": "application/json"}
    request = AWSRequest(method="PUT", url=url, data=body, headers=headers)
    SigV4Auth(credentials, "quicksight", region).add_auth(request)
    response = http.request("PUT", request.url, headers=dict(request.headers), body=request.body)
    response_body = json.loads(response.data.decode("utf-8"))
    if response.status &amp;gt;= 400:
        raise Exception(f"UpdateDataSet failed ({response.status}): {json.dumps(response_body, indent=2)}")
    return response_body

def enrich_dataset(topic_id, dataset_id, aws_account_id, aws_region):
    qs = boto3.client("quicksight", region_name=aws_region)
    print(f"\n{'='*60}")
    print(f"  Dataset Enrichment")
    print(f"{'='*60}")
    print(f"  Topic ID:   {topic_id}")
    print(f"  Dataset ID: {dataset_id}")
    print(f"  Account:    {aws_account_id}")
    print(f"  Region:     {aws_region}")
    print(f"{'='*60}\n")
    # --- Fetch inputs from Quick Sight APIs ---
    print("[1/5] Fetching dataset schema...")
    ds_response = qs.describe_data_set(AwsAccountId=aws_account_id, DataSetId=dataset_id)
    ds = ds_response["DataSet"]
    print(f"      Dataset: {ds['Name']}")
    print(f"      ARN: {ds['Arn']}")
    print("[2/5] Fetching topic metadata...")
    topic_response = qs.describe_topic(AwsAccountId=aws_account_id, TopicId=topic_id)
    topic_raw = topic_response
    print(f"      Topic: {topic_raw['Topic'].get('Name', topic_id)}")
    print(f"      Datasets in topic: {len(topic_raw['Topic']['DataSets'])}")
    # --- Match topic dataset by ARN ---
    target_arn = ds["Arn"]
    topic_datasets = topic_raw["Topic"]["DataSets"]
    topic = None
    for td in topic_datasets:
        if td.get("DatasetArn") == target_arn:
            topic = td
            break
    if topic is None:
        print(f"      WARNING: No topic dataset matched ARN {target_arn}. Falling back to index 0.")
        topic = topic_datasets[0]
    else:
        print(f"      Matched topic dataset by ARN ✓")
    custom_instructions_str = topic_raw.get("CustomInstructions", {}).get("CustomInstructionsString", "")
    # --- Extract dataset structure ---
    physical_table_map = ds["PhysicalTableMap"]
    import_mode = ds.get("ImportMode", "SPICE")
    uses_new_experience = "DataPrepConfiguration" in ds
    print(f"      Experience: {'NEW (DataPrepConfiguration)' if uses_new_experience else 'OLD (LogicalTableMap)'}")
    if uses_new_experience:
        data_prep = ds["DataPrepConfiguration"]
    else:
        logical_table_map = ds["LogicalTableMap"]
    ptable_key = list(physical_table_map.keys())[0]
    # Auto-detect source type
    source_entry = physical_table_map[ptable_key]
    if "RelationalTable" in source_entry:
        input_columns = source_entry["RelationalTable"]["InputColumns"]
        source_type = "RelationalTable"
    elif "S3Source" in source_entry:
        input_columns = source_entry["S3Source"]["InputColumns"]
        source_type = "S3Source"
    elif "CustomSql" in source_entry:
        input_columns = source_entry["CustomSql"]["Columns"]
        source_type = "CustomSql"
    else:
        raise ValueError(f"Unknown physical table type: {list(source_entry.keys())}")
    print(f"      Source type: {source_type}")
    print(f"      Input columns: {len(input_columns)}")
    col_id_map = {col["Name"]: col.get("Id", col["Name"]) for col in input_columns}
    # --- 1. Map column descriptions and synonyms ---
    print("\n[3/5] Building enrichment...")
    column_metadata = []
    for tc in topic["Columns"]:
        col_id = col_id_map.get(tc["ColumnName"])
        if not col_id:
            continue
        column_metadata.append({
            "ColumnNames": [tc["ColumnName"]],
            "ColumnProperties": [{
                "Description": {"Text": tc.get("ColumnDescription", "")},
                "AdditionalNotes": {"Text": ("The synonym for the column is " + ", ".join(tc.get("ColumnSynonyms", []))) if tc.get("ColumnSynonyms") else ""}
            }]
        })
    # --- 2. Add calculated fields ---
    if uses_new_experience:
        dest_key = list(data_prep["DestinationTableMap"].keys())[0]
        last_step = data_prep["DestinationTableMap"][dest_key]["Source"]["TransformOperationId"]
        for step_id, step in data_prep["TransformStepMap"].items():
            if "CastColumnTypesStep" in step:
                for op in step["CastColumnTypesStep"].get("CastColumnTypeOperations", []):
                    op.pop("SubType", None)
        for i, calc in enumerate(topic.get("CalculatedFields", [])):
            col_name = calc["CalculatedFieldName"].upper().replace(" ", "_")
            col_id = f"col-{col_name.lower().replace('_', '-')}"
            step_id = f"topic-calc-step-{i}"
            data_prep["TransformStepMap"][step_id] = {
                "CreateColumnsStep": {
                    "Alias": f"Add {calc['CalculatedFieldName']}",
                    "Columns": [{"ColumnName": col_name, "ColumnId": col_id, "Expression": calc["Expression"]}],
                    "Source": {"TransformOperationId": last_step}
                }
            }
            last_step = step_id
            column_metadata.append({
                "ColumnNames": [col_name],
                "ColumnProperties": [{
                    "Description": {"Text": calc.get("CalculatedFieldDescription", "")},
                    "AdditionalNotes": {"Text": ("The synonym for the column is " + ", ".join(calc.get("CalculatedFieldSynonyms", []))) if calc.get("CalculatedFieldSynonyms") else ""}
                }]
            })
        data_prep["DestinationTableMap"][dest_key]["Source"]["TransformOperationId"] = last_step
    else:
        lt_key = list(logical_table_map.keys())[0]
        lt = logical_table_map[lt_key]
        if "DataTransforms" in lt:
            for transform in lt["DataTransforms"]:
                if "CastColumnTypeOperation" in transform:
                    transform["CastColumnTypeOperation"].pop("SubType", None)
        for i, calc in enumerate(topic.get("CalculatedFields", [])):
            col_name = calc["CalculatedFieldName"].upper().replace(" ", "_")
            col_id = f"col-{col_name.lower().replace('_', '-')}"
            create_op = {"CreateColumnsOperation": {"Columns": [{"ColumnName": col_name, "ColumnId": col_id, "Expression": calc["Expression"]}]}}
            insert_idx = len(lt.get("DataTransforms", []))
            for idx, t in enumerate(lt.get("DataTransforms", [])):
                if "ProjectOperation" in t:
                    insert_idx = idx
                    break
            lt.setdefault("DataTransforms", []).insert(insert_idx, create_op)
            for t in lt.get("DataTransforms", []):
                if "ProjectOperation" in t:
                    t["ProjectOperation"]["ProjectedColumns"].append(col_name)
                    break
            column_metadata.append({
                "ColumnNames": [col_name],
                "ColumnProperties": [{
                    "Description": {"Text": calc.get("CalculatedFieldDescription", "")},
                    "AdditionalNotes": {"Text": ("The synonym for the column is " + ", ".join(calc.get("CalculatedFieldSynonyms", []))) if calc.get("CalculatedFieldSynonyms") else ""}
                }]
            })
    # --- 3. Build CustomInstructions text ---
    lines = []
    if custom_instructions_str:
        lines.append(custom_instructions_str)
    for entity in topic.get("NamedEntities", []):
        synonyms = ", ".join(entity.get("EntitySynonyms", []))
        fields = ", ".join(d.get("FieldName", "") for d in entity.get("Definition", []))
        entry = f"Entity {entity['EntityName']}: {entity.get('EntityDescription', '')}"
        if fields:
            entry += f" (fields: {fields})"
        if synonyms:
            entry += f" (synonyms: {synonyms})"
        lines.append(entry)
    for f in topic.get("Filters", []):
        fname = f.get("FilterName", "Unnamed")
        field = f.get("OperandFieldName", "")
        ftype = f.get("FilterType", "")
        if ftype == "CATEGORY_FILTER":
            cf = f.get("CategoryFilter", {})
            func = cf.get("CategoryFilterFunction", "")
            values = cf.get("Constant", {}).get("CollectiveConstant", {}).get("ValueList", [])
            inverse = cf.get("Inverse", False)
            op = "NOT IN" if inverse else "IN"
            lines.append(f"Business Rule - {fname}: Filter where {field} {func} {op} [{', '.join(values)}].")
        elif ftype == "NUMERIC_RANGE_FILTER":
            nrf = f.get("NumericRangeFilter", {})
            agg = nrf.get("Aggregation", "")
            inclusive = nrf.get("Inclusive", False)
            minimum = nrf.get("Constant", {}).get("RangeConstant", {}).get("Minimum", "")
            maximum = nrf.get("Constant", {}).get("RangeConstant", {}).get("Maximum", "")
            if minimum:
                op2 = "&amp;gt;=" if inclusive else "&amp;gt;"
                lines.append(f"Business Rule - {fname}: Filter where {agg}({field}) {op2} {minimum}.")
            elif maximum:
                op2 = "&amp;lt;=" if inclusive else "&amp;lt;"
                lines.append(f"Business Rule - {fname}: Filter where {agg}({field}) {op2} {maximum}.")
    instruction_text = "\n".join(lines)
    # --- 4. Assemble SemanticModelConfiguration ---
    topic_desc = topic_raw["Topic"].get("Description", "")
    if uses_new_experience:
        dest_key = list(data_prep["DestinationTableMap"].keys())[0]
    else:
        dest_key = lt_key
    semantic_model_config = {
        "TableMap": {
            "semantic-table": {
                "Alias": "Semantic Table",
                "DestinationTableId": dest_key,
                "SemanticMetadata": {"ColumnMetadata": column_metadata}
            }
        },
    }
    if topic_desc or instruction_text:
        semantic_entry = {}
        if topic_desc:
            semantic_entry["Description"] = {"Text": topic_desc}
        if instruction_text:
            semantic_entry["CustomInstructions"] = [{"InlineCustomInstruction": {"InstructionText": instruction_text}}]
        semantic_model_config["SemanticMetadata"] = [semantic_entry]
    # --- Print enrichment summary ---
    print(f"      Columns enriched: {len(column_metadata)}")
    print(f"      Calculated fields added: {len(topic.get('CalculatedFields', []))}")
    print(f"      Named entities: {len(topic.get('NamedEntities', []))}")
    print(f"      Filters as rules: {len(topic.get('Filters', []))}")
    print(f"      Custom instructions: {len(instruction_text)} chars")
    # --- 5. Build and apply update payload ---
    update_payload = {
        "Name": ds["Name"],
        "PhysicalTableMap": physical_table_map,
        "ImportMode": import_mode,
        "SemanticModelConfiguration": semantic_model_config
    }
    if uses_new_experience:
        update_payload["DataPrepConfiguration"] = data_prep
    else:
        update_payload["LogicalTableMap"] = logical_table_map
    print("\n[4/5] Applying update via SigV4 API call...")
    update_response = raw_update_data_set(aws_account_id, dataset_id, update_payload, aws_region)
    print(f"\n[5/5] SUCCESS!")
    print(f"      Dataset '{ds['Name']}' enriched with Topic metadata.")
    print(f"      Status: {update_response.get('Status', 'OK')}")
    print(f"      Ingestion: {update_response.get('IngestionId', 'N/A')}")

if __name__ == "__main__":
    if len(sys.argv) &amp;lt; 5:
        print(__doc__)
        sys.exit(1)
    topic_id = sys.argv[1]
    dataset_id = sys.argv[2]
    aws_account_id = sys.argv[3]
    aws_region = sys.argv[4]
    try:
        enrich_dataset(topic_id, dataset_id, aws_account_id, aws_region)
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"\n ERROR: {e}")
        sys.exit(1)&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="run-the-script"&gt;3.2 Run the script&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Execute the script, passing your four parameters (Topic ID, Dataset ID, Account ID, and Region):&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;python enrich_dataset.py &amp;lt;topic_id&amp;gt; &amp;lt;dataset_id&amp;gt; &amp;lt;aws_account_id&amp;gt; &amp;lt;aws_region&amp;gt;&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="expected-output"&gt;3.3 Expected output&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;After the script completes, confirm that the enrichment was applied correctly by inspecting the dataset through the Quick Sight API or in the Quick Sight console:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src="https://d2908q01vomqb2.cloudfront.net/f1f836cb4ea6efb2a0b1b99f41ad8b103eff4b59/2026/07/06/ML-21217-5.jpg" alt="Enriched dataset semantic metadata shown in the console after the script runs" width="800"&gt;&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="verify-the-output-file"&gt;3.4 Verify the output file&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;div class="hide-language"&gt; 
 &lt;pre&gt;&lt;code class="language-bash"&gt;# Check the three sections exist:
python -c "import json; d=json.load(open('enriched-payload.json')); print(list(d.keys()))"
# Expected: ['physical_table_map', 'data_prep_configuration', 'semantic_model_configuration']&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/pre&gt; 
&lt;/div&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;code&gt;physical_table_map&lt;/code&gt;: unchanged from the source dataset.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;code&gt;data_prep_configuration&lt;/code&gt;: with an added &lt;code&gt;CreateColumnsStep&lt;/code&gt; for calculated fields.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;code&gt;semantic_model_configuration&lt;/code&gt;: with column descriptions, synonyms, and custom instructions (formulas, entities, and business rules).&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="validation-best-practices"&gt;Validation best practices&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Across all scenarios, we recommend the following validation approach:&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Build a validation question set.&lt;/strong&gt; Before migration, document 10–20 natural language questions that work correctly against your current legacy Topic. These become your regression test suite.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Run side-by-side comparisons.&lt;/strong&gt; Ask the same questions against the legacy Topic (before removal) and against the enriched dataset. Results should match.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Test edge cases.&lt;/strong&gt; Try ambiguous questions, synonym variations, and multi-step queries. The enriched dataset should handle these as effectively as (or better than) the legacy Topic did.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Validate with actual users.&lt;/strong&gt; Have 2–3 business users test the enriched dataset chat experience before you retire the legacy Topic. Their natural phrasing may reveal gaps in your synonym or entity coverage.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Document behavioral differences.&lt;/strong&gt; Some behaviors will differ between legacy Topic-based querying and dataset enrichment querying: 
  &lt;ul&gt; 
   &lt;li&gt;Legacy Topic filters are pre-defined and selectable; business rules in custom instructions require the user to reference them by name or intent.&lt;/li&gt; 
   &lt;li&gt;Legacy Topic calculated fields support aggregation-level expressions; dataset calculated fields operate row-by-row (aggregations happen at query time).&lt;/li&gt; 
   &lt;li&gt;Named entities in legacy Topics have structured metadata; in dataset enrichment they’re text-based instructions.&lt;/li&gt; 
  &lt;/ul&gt; &lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="key-considerations"&gt;Key considerations&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;ul&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;The &lt;code&gt;update-data-set&lt;/code&gt; API requires a full replacement of all configuration on every call. Incremental updates to individual columns aren’t supported.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Named entities and filters don’t have dedicated API fields in &lt;code&gt;SemanticModelConfiguration&lt;/code&gt;. They must be expressed as text within &lt;code&gt;CustomInstructions&lt;/code&gt;.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Not all legacy Topic calculation expressions translate directly to dataset-level expressions. Aggregation-based calculations may need restructuring.&lt;/li&gt; 
 &lt;li&gt;Currently, you can’t upgrade legacy datasets in-place. You must create a new dataset with the new data prep experience to enrich it.&lt;/li&gt; 
&lt;/ul&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="clean-up"&gt;Clean up&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To avoid incurring ongoing charges, delete the AWS resources (IAM roles, Quick Sight data sources, policies) that you created as part of experimentation. For instructions, see the &lt;a href="https://docs.aws.amazon.com/quick/latest/userguide/what-is.html" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Quick documentation&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;h3 id="conclusion"&gt;Conclusion&lt;/h3&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;Dataset Enrichment in Amazon Quick moves business intelligence metadata from a separate legacy Topic layer into the dataset itself. One asset carries its own business meaning: column descriptions, synonyms, calculation logic, entity definitions, and business rules, all governed in one place.&lt;/p&gt; 
&lt;p&gt;To get started, identify your highest-value dataset, apply enrichment using the patterns in this post, and test it in Amazon Quick chat. For the full &lt;code&gt;SemanticModelConfiguration&lt;/code&gt; API reference, see the Amazon Quick documentation. For questions and community discussion, visit the &lt;a href="https://community.amazonquicksight.com/" target="_blank" rel="noopener"&gt;Amazon Quick Community.&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;

					<description><![CDATA[<p>Introducing OpenAI's latest frontier model series, the Asia Pacific Data Zone, and product agent capabilities, all generally available in Microsoft Foundry.</p>
<p>The post <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/blog/frontier-models-and-production-agents-advancing-microsoft-foundry-for-the-agentic-era/">Frontier models and production agents: Advancing Microsoft Foundry for the agentic era</a> appeared first on <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/blog">Microsoft Azure Blog</a>.</p>
]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[
<div class="section wp-block-bloginabox-theme-section has-global-padding is-layout-constrained wp-block-bloginabox-theme-section-is-layout-constrained">
	
<div
	class="section__background alignfull is-layout-constrained"
	>

		
		
		
		</div>
<div class="section__content alignfull is-layout-constrained">
	

<p class="has-white-color has-electric-blue-400-background-color has-text-color has-background has-link-color wp-elements-5c53060b23b0e375f0d8bd41d1502d61 wp-block-paragraph"><strong>What&#8217;s new today</strong><br>GPT‑5.6 is generally available in Microsoft Foundry, alongside the Asia-Pacific Data Zone, and hosted agents in Foundry Agent Service.</p>

</div>
</div>



<hr class="wp-block-separator has-alpha-channel-opacity" />



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">AI only creates value when it shows up in real systems—systems that are reliable, observable, and aligned to business outcomes. More than 100,000 organizations are already building on <a href="https://ai.azure.com/home" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Microsoft Foundry</a>, and companies like <strong>Adobe, Telefónica, and Tata Consultancy Services</strong> are running agents in production today.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">At Microsoft Build, we laid out a simple promise for the agentic era: developers should be able to build an agent where they already work, run it on infrastructure they can trust, and put it in front of the people who need it— without stitching together disconnected platforms. Today, that vision moves from roadmap to reality with three sets of updates <strong>now</strong> <strong>generally available</strong> in Microsoft Foundry:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>OpenAI’s latest frontier model series</strong>: GPT-5.6 Sol, GPT-5.6 Terra, and GPT-5.6—each tuned to a different workload, available in Standard Global and Standard Data Zones.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Asia-Pacific Data Zone</strong>, giving APAC customers a regional option to run frontier OpenAI models while keeping data processing within the region.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Production agents</strong> in Foundry Agent Service, with <strong>hosted agents</strong>, <strong>toolboxes</strong>, and <strong>publishing to Microsoft 365 Copilot and Microsoft Teams</strong>.</li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Together, these capabilities bring frontier models, production agent runtime, enterprise-grade identity, security, and compliance controls, and distribution across Microsoft 365 into a single platform—helping organizations move from experimentation to production without assembling disconnected tools and services.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="why-foundry-is-the-best-agent-platform">Why Foundry is the best agent platform</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph"><a href="https://ai.azure.com/home" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Microsoft Foundry</a> is Microsoft&#8217;s end-to-end platform for building, running, governing, and distributing AI agents. Foundry brings together the capabilities organizations need to move agents into production across three pillars:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Build</strong>: Open and flexible across models and frameworks.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Generate</strong>: Connected to enterprise data, tools, and users.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Govern</strong>: Secured, managed, and optimized for long-term value.</li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">These pillars come together in the latest Foundry updates, helping organizations build, run, and scale production agents on a single platform.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-bloginabox-theme-ump-embed wp-block-msx-ump-embed" class="wp-block-bloginabox-theme-ump-embed">
	<div class="wp-block-embed__wrapper">

<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="build-with-any-framework-and-model-on-the-industry-s-end-to-end-ai-platform">Build with any framework and model on the industry’s end-to-end AI platform</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Agent development starts where developers already work—in GitHub Copilot and Microsoft Visual Studio (VS) Code—with the <a href="https://code.visualstudio.com/docs/intelligentapps/overview" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Foundry Toolkit for VS Code</a> and the <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/foundry/how-to/develop/use-microsoft-foundry-skill?tabs=vscode%2Cfoundry-skill-alone" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Foundry skill</a> handling deployment to Foundry. Whether teams build with <a href="https://github.com/microsoft/agent-framework" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Microsoft Agent Framework</a>, <a href="https://docs.github.com/en/copilot/how-tos/copilot-sdk/getting-started" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">GitHub Copilot SDK</a> (generally available), or Claude Agent SDK, Foundry is the production destination—and it all starts with the right model.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading" id="start-with-the-right-model-for-the-right-job">Start with the right model for the right job</h3>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">An agent is only as capable as the model reasoning behind it. Microsoft Foundry gives organizations access to industry-leading frontier, open-source, and task-specific models through a single platform, allowing teams to choose the right model for every workload.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Today, we&#8217;re making OpenAI&#8217;s GPT-5.6 series generally available in Microsoft Foundry Models and Microsoft Foundry Agent Service:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>GPT-5.6 Sol</strong> delivers the most advanced reasoning capabilities yet, supporting extended reasoning, agentic workflows, and code-focused scenarios, for the most demanding enterprise workloads.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>GPT-5.6 Terra</strong> is a balanced model for everyday work, delivering performance competitive with GPT-5.5 at a lower cost, making it ideal for scaling intelligent applications across the enterprise.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>GPT-5.6 Luna</strong> is the fastest and most affordable model in the family, making it well suited to high-volume, latency-sensitive workloads.</li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Together, the GPT-5.6 series gives organizations the flexibility to match model capability, cost, and performance requirements to specific business scenarios, rather than forcing every workload onto a single model.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Customers consistently tell us that access to new models matters as much as model quality. That’s why we are making <strong>GPT-5.6</strong> available through <strong>Global Standard and Global Priority Processing for all the existing 28 global regions, Data Zones Standard, and Global Provisioned from day one</strong>. This enables customers to adopt the latest frontier AI innovations where they have already built, deployed, and scaled their applications.</p>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading" id="gpt-5-6-pricing">GPT 5.6 pricing</h3>



<figure class="wp-block-table alignleft is-style-regular has-h-5-font-size"><table class="has-fixed-layout"><tbody><tr><td class="has-text-align-center" data-align="center" rowspan="2">Model </td><td class="has-text-align-center" data-align="center" rowspan="2">Deployment </td><td class="has-text-align-center" data-align="center" colspan="2">Pricing (USD $/million tokens) </td></tr><tr><td class="has-text-align-center" data-align="center"><strong>Input</strong> </td><td class="has-text-align-center" data-align="center"><strong>Output</strong> </td></tr><tr><td class="has-text-align-center" data-align="center"><strong>GPT-5.6 Sol</strong> </td><td class="has-text-align-center" data-align="center">Standard Global </td><td class="has-text-align-center" data-align="center">5.00 </td><td class="has-text-align-center" data-align="center">30.00 </td></tr><tr><td class="has-text-align-center" data-align="center"><strong>GPT-5.6 Terra</strong> </td><td class="has-text-align-center" data-align="center">Standard Global </td><td class="has-text-align-center" data-align="center">2.50 </td><td class="has-text-align-center" data-align="center">15.00 </td></tr><tr><td class="has-text-align-center" data-align="center"><strong>GPT-5.6 Luna</strong> </td><td class="has-text-align-center" data-align="center">Standard Global </td><td class="has-text-align-center" data-align="center">1.00 </td><td class="has-text-align-center" data-align="center">6.00 </td></tr></tbody></table></figure>



<h3 class="wp-block-heading" id="run-frontier-ai-where-your-business-operates">Run frontier AI where your business operates</h3>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">More models, in more regions, are only half of what platform expansion means. The other half is more places to run them compliantly. This is exactly what the Asia-Pacific Data Zone delivers. Today we&#8217;re also announcing the <strong>general availability of the Asia-Pacific (APAC) Data Zone for Microsoft Foundry</strong>, enabling APAC customers to run frontier OpenAI models while keeping data processing within the Asia-Pacific regions, with no separate environment to stitch together and no waiting for capability to catch up.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">With Global, Data Zone, and Regional deployment options available in Foundry, organizations can align AI adoption with their sovereignty, compliance, performance, and scale requirements while maintaining a consistent development and operations experience across environments.</p>



<blockquote class="wp-block-quote has-quote-default-font-size is-layout-flow wp-block-quote-is-layout-flow">
<p class="wp-block-paragraph">As financial institutions adopt AI, responsible data handling becomes foundational to trust. Microsoft Foundry’s APAC Data Zone allows us to keep data processing regionally anchored while accessing advanced AI models at scale. This gives us the confidence to accelerate AI innovation responsibly and reinforces our ambition to be a leading AI-powered financial platform in Asia.</p>
<cite>—Hongsoo Kim, Chief Data and AI Officer (CDAO), Viva Republica (Toss)</cite></blockquote>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="generate-impact-with-action-oriented-context-aware-agents">Generate impact with action-oriented, context aware agents</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">A&nbsp;capable model is only the starting point.&nbsp;To put one to work&nbsp;in production,&nbsp;an agent&nbsp;also&nbsp;needs&nbsp;somewhere to run, knowledge of your business, governed access to its tools, memory that carries across interactions&nbsp;and acts on real-world events, and a path to the people who use it.&nbsp;Foundry provides each of&nbsp;these&nbsp;as a&nbsp;built-in&nbsp;capability,&nbsp;designed to&nbsp;work&nbsp;together.</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Where it lives: </strong><a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/foundry/agents/concepts/hosted-agents" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener"><strong>Hosted agents</strong></a><strong>&nbsp;in Foundry Agent Service is now generally available,&nbsp;</strong>giving developers&nbsp;one production runtime for agents built with any framework&nbsp;and harness—Microsoft Agent Framework, GitHub Copilot SDK, LangGraph,&nbsp;OpenClaw,&nbsp;Hermes,&nbsp;and others. It&#8217;s enterprise-ready on day one:&nbsp;<strong>Network&nbsp;isolation&nbsp;with&nbsp;Microsoft Azure Virtual Network (VNet) integration</strong>&nbsp;keeps&nbsp;agent traffic inside your security boundary. For long-running workloads, the new&nbsp;<strong>resilient&nbsp;task support in hosted agents&nbsp;(</strong><a href="https://aka.ms/ResilientAgent/PrPr" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener"><strong>private&nbsp;preview</strong></a><strong>)</strong>&nbsp;makes&nbsp;it&nbsp;easier to build agents that survive failures. The platform provides primitives to keep the sandbox running,&nbsp;your harness provides checkpointing, and together they allow an&nbsp;agent to resume when it restarts. The result: multi-turn conversations, reasoning loops, and human-in-the-loop approvals can pick up where they left off, without developers having to build their own recovery, retry, and state-management.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>How it talks: </strong><a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/ai-services/speech-service/how-to-voice-live-hosted-agent-integration" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener"><strong>Hosted agents with Voice Live</strong></a><strong> is now generally available.</strong> Developers can add&nbsp;real-time voice experiences to the agents they built with the frameworks they prefer, using the Azure VoiceLive SDK.&nbsp;</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>What it knows</strong>: Foundry<strong>&nbsp;</strong>gives agents access to enterprise knowledge without&nbsp;requiring&nbsp;developers to&nbsp;build a&nbsp;complex&nbsp;retrieval pipeline from scratch. Microsoft IQ brings together <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/microsoft-365/copilot/extensibility/work-iq/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener"><strong>Work IQ</strong></a> for real-time awareness of your Microsoft 365 environment, <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/fabric/iq/overview" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener"><strong>Fabric IQ</strong></a> for your structured data, and <a href="https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/webiq" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener"><strong>Web IQ</strong></a> for low-latency live web grounding—all unified behind <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/foundry/agents/concepts/what-is-foundry-iq?tabs=portal" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener"><strong>Foundry IQ</strong></a>, now generally available as the SLA-backed knowledge layer behind every Foundry agent.&nbsp;</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>How it reaches its tools: </strong><a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/foundry/agents/how-to/tools/toolbox?pivots=python" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener"><strong>Toolboxes in Foundry</strong></a><strong> is generally available. </strong>Instead of shipping every tool definition on every request, a toolbox dynamically selects the right tool for the job—giving agents governed, curated access while dramatically reducing the token overhead of large tool sets.&nbsp;</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>How it&nbsp;remembers&nbsp;and&nbsp;responds to the world: </strong><a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/foundry/agents/concepts/what-is-memory?tabs=conversational-agent" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener"><strong>Memory</strong></a><strong>&nbsp;and&nbsp;</strong><a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/foundry/agents/concepts/routines" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener"><strong>routines</strong></a><strong>&nbsp;in Foundry Agent Service are in public preview.&nbsp;</strong>Memory (procedural, user, and session) lets agents carry context across interactions. Routines run any agent on a recurring schedule or timer so it acts without a user prompt;&nbsp;new event-based triggers, powered by the connector gateway, now let the same agent wake up the moment an upstream system signals a change—a ticket filed, a file landing in storage, a workflow completing.&nbsp;</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>How it reaches users: </strong><a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/foundry/agents/how-to/publish-copilot" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener"><strong>Publishing to Microsoft Teams and Microsoft 365 Copilot</strong></a><strong> is generally available next week.</strong>&nbsp;The agents your developers build land in the applications where&nbsp;hundreds&nbsp;of millions of people already do their work with identity, permissions, and policy flowing through automatically.&nbsp;And&nbsp;it works even for network-isolated agents: when a project runs behind a private endpoint, you publish through a <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/foundry/agents/how-to/publish-copilot-virtual-network?view=foundry" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener"><strong>documented flow</strong></a> rather than the one-click button. The agent stays on your private network while Microsoft&#8217;s channel adapters reach it through your own firewall and reverse proxy.</li>
</ul>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="govern-and-optimize-the-full-ai-lifecycle-with-observability-and-controls">Govern&nbsp;and&nbsp;optimize&nbsp;the full AI lifecycle with observability and controls</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">An agent you&nbsp;can&#8217;t&nbsp;see,&nbsp;improve, or secure is an agent you&nbsp;can&#8217;t&nbsp;put&nbsp;in production—so Foundry treats trust as a platform priority, not a developer responsibility. What&#8217;s&nbsp;new in this release closes the loop around everything that happens after you&nbsp;build: seeing what an agent did, making it better, and proving it&#8217;s worth running.</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>How you see what it did—</strong><a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/foundry/observability/quickstarts/quickstart-tracing-hosted-agent?tabs=azd" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener"><strong>tracing</strong></a><strong>&nbsp;and&nbsp;</strong><a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/foundry/observability/quickstarts/quickstart-evaluate-hosted-agent?tabs=azd" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener"><strong>evaluation</strong></a><strong>&nbsp;for hosted agents, generally available. </strong>See exactly what an agent did, why, and where it went wrong, and evaluate behavior systematically before and after you ship.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>How it gets better and cheaper</strong>—<a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/foundry/agents/quickstarts/quickstart-optimize-hosted-agent" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener"><strong>agent optimizer in Foundry Agent Service</strong></a>, in public preview. It tests your prompts, skills, models, and tools together and automatically&nbsp;identifies&nbsp;better configurations—often letting you hold quality while moving to a smaller, cheaper model.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>How you prove its value</strong>—<a href="https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/blog/azure-ai-foundry-blog/introducing-roi-for-agents-in-foundry/4531970" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener"><strong>ROI for agents in Microsoft Foundry</strong></a>, in private preview. It connects an agent&#8217;s traces, business-value evaluations, and operating cost into a single view—surfacing KPIs like net value, total cost, and current ROI in the dashboard, so&nbsp;customers&nbsp;can see whether a production agent is creating more value than it costs to run, and drill into traces when it isn&#8217;t.&nbsp;</li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">As agents scale from pilots to thousands of runs a day, Foundry gives teams the levers to keep spend predictable without leaving the platform.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">That starts with choice.</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Choose how you deploy.</strong> Foundry offers Global, Data Zone, and Regional deployments,&nbsp;so you can align AI to your sovereignty, compliance, and performance requirements, running frontier models while keeping data processing in-region.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Choose how you pay for&nbsp;model&nbsp;inference.</strong> A full spectrum of offers—Standard, Priority Processing, Provisioned Throughput, and Batch—lets you optimize for agility, latency, throughput, and cost on one platform.</li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">On top of that foundation,&nbsp;<a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/foundry/openai/concepts/model-router" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"><strong>model&nbsp;router</strong></a> matches each request to the right model, <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/foundry/openai/how-to/prompt-caching" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"><strong>prompt&nbsp;caching</strong></a> cuts redundant computation,&nbsp;and <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/foundry/openai/how-to/spillover-traffic-management?tabs=portal" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"><strong>PTU&nbsp;spillover</strong></a> and&nbsp;quota optimization preserve service continuity through usage spikes. For agents,&nbsp;<strong>toolboxes in Foundry</strong> send only the tools each request needs,&nbsp;and&nbsp;<strong>agent optimizer</strong>&nbsp;tunes prompts, skills, tools, and model choice against your own evaluators.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">And spend is only half the equation.&nbsp;<strong>ROI for agents in Foundry</strong>&nbsp;connects business value, usage, and cost in one view—so teams can see whether a production agent is creating more value than it costs to run, and where cost is outpacing value.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">For a hands-on walkthrough, watch our new&nbsp;<a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=mB0IyELzjRg" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Microsoft Mechanics episode</a>&nbsp;on token economics for agents.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="in-production-what-teams-are-building-on-foundry">In production: What teams are&nbsp;building on Foundry&nbsp;&nbsp;</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">The organizations&nbsp;building on&nbsp;Foundry&nbsp;aren&#8217;t&nbsp;experimenting; they&#8217;re&nbsp;shipping, from digital natives to the world&#8217;s largest enterprises.</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Adobe</strong>&nbsp;is building with GitHub, Foundry Agent Service, and Azure Functions, deploying agents&nbsp;for their applications and saving time and work getting to production.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Telefónica</strong>&nbsp;has adopted Microsoft Foundry as the core of their corporate agentic platform, with the first wave of agents tackling network operations—a telco’s most complex, strategic domain—across Microsoft Agent Framework, hosted agents, AI Gateway, and&nbsp;Azure&nbsp;Logic Apps.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Tata Consultancy Services</strong>&nbsp;is using agent optimizer in Foundry Agent Service to improve agent performance with a more structured approach to prompt tuning, helping teams reduce manual effort while measuring gains in task adherence and execution efficiency.</li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">The pattern is consistent: teams that once spent weeks integrating, securing, and deploying agents are now doing it in days, on infrastructure that meets their compliance bar, reaching users through tools they already trust.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="get-started">Get started</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Everything&nbsp;in this post is live in&nbsp;<a href="https://ai.azure.com/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Microsoft Foundry</a>.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Follow the <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/foundry/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">documentation</a> and Microsoft Learn courses. Developers can get started in minutes by following the <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/foundry/agents/quickstarts/quickstart-hosted-agent?pivots=azd" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Quickstart</a>, which walks through setting up, testing, and deploying a production-ready hosted&nbsp;agent&nbsp;end to end.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Check out <a href="https://github.com/microsoft/ai-agents-for-beginners" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">AI Agents for Beginners</a> for a 12-lesson curriculum, then go deeper with guided labs: <a href="https://microsoftlearning.github.io/mslearn-ai-agents/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Develop AI Agents in Azure</a>, <a href="https://github.com/microsoft/Hosted_Agents_Workshop_dotNET" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Hosted Agents Workshop (.NET)</a>,&nbsp;the&nbsp;<a href="https://github.com/microsoft-foundry/Foundry_Toolkit_for_VSCode_Lab/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Foundry Toolkit for VS Code and hosted agents workshop</a>,&nbsp;and the <a href="https://github.com/microsoft/Learn-Microsoft-Agent-Framework-with-Foundry-ZavaShop-Supply-Chain-Workshop" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">ZavaShop Supply Chain Workshop</a>.&nbsp;To put your agents on a solid quality footing, read <a href="https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/blog/azure-ai-foundry-blog/evaluating-ai-agents-a-practical-guide-with-microsoft-foundry/4500224" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Evaluating AI Agents: A Practical Guide with Microsoft Foundry</a>.&nbsp;</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph"><strong>Watch:</strong> <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=iR7_57lJOz8" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Foundry Agent Service + Microsoft Agent Framework Explained</a>—Jeff Hollan walks through how to operationalize AI agents from deployment to real-world impact.</p>

										<content:encoded><![CDATA[

		</svg>
	</button>
	<div id="accordion-collapse-507c5bb2-76b3-42b0-a862-223ebd7f186c" class="table-of-contents-block__collapse-wrapper collapse show" data-parent="#accordion-507c5bb2-76b3-42b0-a862-223ebd7f186c">
		<div class="table-of-contents-block__content">
			<ol class="table-of-contents-block__list"><li class="table-of-contents-block__list-item"><a class="table-of-contents-block__list-item-link" href="#resiliency-as-a-shared-responsibility-not-a-handoff">Resiliency as a shared responsibility, not a handoff</a></li><li class="table-of-contents-block__list-item"><a class="table-of-contents-block__list-item-link" href="#platform-foundations-that-reflect-reality-zones-regions-and-sovereignty">Platform foundations that reflect reality: zones, regions, and sovereignty</a></li><li class="table-of-contents-block__list-item"><a class="table-of-contents-block__list-item-link" href="#azure-features-and-capabilities-strengthen-resiliency-outcomes">Azure features and capabilities strengthen resiliency outcomes</a></li><li class="table-of-contents-block__list-item"><a class="table-of-contents-block__list-item-link" href="#bridging-intent-to-execution-through-experiences-on-azure">Bridging intent to execution through experiences on Azure</a></li><li class="table-of-contents-block__list-item"><a class="table-of-contents-block__list-item-link" href="#how-you-can-build-resilience-in-azure">How you can build Resilience in Azure</a></li><li class="table-of-contents-block__list-item"><a class="table-of-contents-block__list-item-link" href="#azure-essentials">Azure Essentials</a></li></ol>		</div>
	</div>
	<span class="table-of-contents-block__progress-bar"></span>
</aside>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Resiliency in the cloud is often described in terms of availability, such as how quickly a system fails over, how many replicas exist, or what a service-level agreement guarantees. But for most organizations today, especially those operating in regulated, sovereign, or geopolitically sensitive environments, resiliency is something far more fundamental. It is the ability to continue operating under pressure, protect what matters most, and recover safely when the unexpected happens.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">A useful way to think about this is not a system problem, but a city problem. A modern city does not depend on a single power source, a single road, or a single control system. It is designed to withstand disruptions, whether from infrastructure failures, natural events, or security incidents. It has redundancy—but more importantly—it has governance, control, and recovery mechanisms that reflect local realities. <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/solutions/resiliency" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Cloud resiliency</a> operates in much the same way. It is not just about avoiding outages; it is about ensuring systems can adapt, recover, and keep functioning within real-world constraints.</p>



<div class="wp-block-buttons is-content-justification-center is-layout-flex wp-container-core-buttons-is-layout-a89b3969 wp-block-buttons-is-layout-flex">
<div class="wp-block-button"><a class="wp-block-button__link wp-element-button" href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/solutions/resiliency" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Get started with Resiliency in Azure</a></div>
</div>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">On Azure, resiliency is not something Microsoft delivers <em>to</em> customers. It is something Microsoft builds <em>with</em> them. The platform provides deeply resilient infrastructure and increasingly intelligent capabilities, but resiliency outcomes only emerge when those are intentionally designed, aligned with sovereignty constraints, and continuously validated against real-world conditions. Last year, we explained how at its core, <a href="https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/blog/azuregovernanceandmanagementblog/improve-your-resiliency-posture-with-new-capabilities-and-intelligent-assistance/4469771" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Azure approaches resiliency across three interconnected pillars</a>: <strong>infrastructure resiliency, data resiliency, and cyber recovery.</strong></p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Infrastructure resiliency</strong>: ensuring applications remain available through failure conditions.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Data resiliency</strong>: ensuring data remains protected, durable, and recoverable.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Cyber recovery</strong>: ensuring organizations can recover safely from compromised states.</li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Together, they ensure not only that systems remain available, but that they remain <strong>recoverable and trustworthy</strong>—even when failure modes are unpredictable. These pillars are operationalized through a lifecycle approach that helps organizations design, improve, and continuously validate their resiliency posture.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">What differentiates Azure is how these elements come together. Azure provides not just <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/solutions/resiliency" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">resilient infrastructure</a>, but a unified approach that spans platform capabilities, observability, validation, and intelligent remediation, allowing organizations to move from designing for resiliency to continuously operating and improving it.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="resiliency-as-a-shared-responsibility-not-a-handoff">Resiliency as a shared responsibility, not a handoff</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">In any city, infrastructure providers ensure that roads, utilities, and foundational systems are reliable. But how buildings are designed, how emergency plans are executed, and how critical services are protected; those remain the responsibility of the city and its operators.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Azure’s shared responsibility model follows the same principle. Microsoft is responsible for delivering a <strong>resilient cloud platform foundation like </strong>regions, physical datacenters, networking, isolation boundaries, and engineering systems that reduce blast radius and improve durability at scale. This includes capabilities such as Availability Zones, regional isolation, and services like <strong><a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/products/backup" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Azure Backup</a> and <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/products/site-recovery" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Azure Site Recovery</a></strong>. Customers then build on Azure enabled experiences to configure the right capabilities and achieve their desired resiliency outcomes. This includes how applications are architected, how dependencies are managed, how recovery objectives are defined, and how backup and disaster recovery are configured and tested. In sovereign and regulated environments, this responsibility becomes even more critical where customers explicitly define where data resides, how it moves, and how recovery aligns with compliance and jurisdictional requirements.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="platform-foundations-that-reflect-reality-zones-regions-and-sovereignty">Platform foundations that reflect reality: zones, regions, and sovereignty</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Modern <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/solutions/resiliency" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Azure resiliency</a> starts with a <strong>zone-first design approach</strong>, where applications are built to tolerate the loss of an entire Availability Zone. This significantly reduces the likelihood of localized infrastructure failures impacting application availability.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">However, resilience does not stop at zones. Regions themselves are not uniform, and assuming uniformity is one of the most common causes of design fragility.</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item">Some Azure regions are <strong>paired</strong>, with predefined recovery regions aligned for disaster recovery.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Others are <strong>non-paired</strong>, often due to sovereignty, regulatory, or geographic constraints.</li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">This distinction fundamentally shapes resiliency architecture.</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Paired region scenario (predictable recovery): </strong>Azure provides a spectrum of durability options from locally redundant storage (LRS) to zone redundant (ZRS) and geo‑redundant storage (GRS), enabling customers to align data protection strategies with their availability, compliance, and data sovereignty requirements. For example, a financial services application deployed in West Europe can leverage its paired region (North Europe) for disaster recovery. Using Azure Site Recovery (ASR), workloads are continuously replicated and orchestrated to enable application-level continuity during a regional disruption. 
<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item">The predefined region pairing offers predictable failover behavior, along with well-understood Recovery Point Objective (RPO) and Recovery Time Objective (RTO) trade-offs. However, modern Azure resiliency guidance has evolved beyond strict reliance on region pairs. As outlined in the <a href="https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/blog/reliability-and-resiliency-in-azure/modern-azure-resilience-with-mark-russinovich/4508967">Modern Azure Resilience with Mark Russinovich</a> blog, customers are increasingly adopting flexible multi-region architectures, including non-paired region strategies based on factors such as service availability, capacity, latency, and data residency requirements. These patterns emphasize that disaster recovery is no longer bound to predefined pairs, but instead is a design choice aligned to workload-specific needs.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">In such scenarios, Azure Site Recovery plays a critical role by providing consistent, application-aware replication and failover orchestration across any chosen region, paired or not. This allows customers to standardize their recovery strategy while retaining the flexibility to meet evolving business, regulatory, and scale considerations.</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Non-paired region scenario (sovereign constraint): </strong>a government workload operates in a sovereign region with no predefined pair. Cross-region recovery is restricted. The architecture prioritizes zonal high availability and restore-based recovery using backup to region of choice, ensuring data remains within jurisdictional boundaries. Recovery is slower but fully compliant.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Asymmetric recovery scenario (regulated enterprise): </strong>a multinational enterprise deploys in a constrained geography where only subsets of data can leave the region. For example, Azure Site Recovery enables failover for critical services, while sensitive data relies on Azure Backup for in-boundary recovery. The result is an intentionally asymmetric resiliency model, balancing compliance with business continuity.</li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">The result is a shift from one-size-fits-all architectures to workload-driven resiliency design, where recovery strategies are intentionally aligned to business, regulatory, and operational constraints.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="azure-features-and-capabilities-strengthen-resiliency-outcomes">Azure features and capabilities strengthen resiliency outcomes</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph"><a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/solutions/resiliency" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Resiliency in Azure</a> is not delivered by a single service, but it is achieved through a <strong>set of capabilities and services</strong>. These capabilities work together to ensure applications remain available, data remains protected, and systems can recover even under infrastructure failures, regional disruptions, or cyber-attacks. It begins with <strong>zone-resilient foundations</strong> that reduce exposure to localized failures, and extends through <strong>autoscaling, load balancing, and health-aware traffic management</strong> that keeps applications responsive under stress. </p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">For broader infrastructure or regional disruptions, <strong>Azure Site Recovery</strong> enables continuity through replication and failover orchestration. Equally important, <strong>Azure Backup</strong> addresses a different class of risk like corruption, accidental deletion, compliance retention, and cyber compromise by enabling recovery to a trusted point in time when failover is not enough. These capabilities are most effective when paired with <strong>strong observability and rehydration-friendly design</strong>, where systems can detect issues early, recover automatically, and rebuild quickly. The result is a more complete view of resiliency: not just maintaining uptime but sustaining trust and recoverability under real-world failure conditions.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="bridging-intent-to-execution-through-experiences-on-azure">Bridging intent to execution through experiences on Azure</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Customers had tools but lacked a unified way to measure and improve their resiliency posture. Introduced at Microsoft Build 2026 and available in public preview, <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/resiliency/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"><strong>Azure Infrastructure Resiliency Manager</strong></a> addresses this challenge. It provides an <strong>application-centric and resource-centric view of resiliency</strong>, bringing together <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/solutions/resiliency" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Resiliency in Azure</a>, <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/products/advisor" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Azure Advisor</a>, <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/products/chaos-studio" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Azure Chaos Studio</a>, and <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/products/monitor" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Azure Monitor</a> into a single, cohesive experience. </p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">A key starting point is <strong>zonal resiliency posture</strong>. It helps customers understand whether their workloads are truly zone-resilient, identify hidden dependencies, and pinpoint gaps between intended architecture and actual deployment.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">It introduces a lifecycle approach to resiliency:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Start resilient</strong>: design workloads with the right foundational posture.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Get resilient</strong>: identify and close gaps in existing systems.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Stay resilient:</strong> continuously validate and improve through drills and monitoring.</li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">At the core of Azure Infrastructure Resiliency Manager is the <strong><a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/copilot/resiliency-agent" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Resiliency Agent</a></strong>, which brings intelligence and automation into the lifecycle. The agent evaluates workloads holistically and identifies risks, surfaces misconfigurations, and explains trade-offs across cost, availability, and compliance. But its role extends beyond analysis. This represents a shift from reactive guidance to proactive and increasingly autonomous resiliency management.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">In addition to guiding remediation, the Resiliency Agent can generate <strong>Infrastructure-as-Code (IaC) templates</strong>, enabling teams to directly implement recommended changes in their deployment pipelines. This is a fundamental shift: resiliency moves from being <strong>advisory to executable</strong>. It becomes embedded in DevOps workflows; codified, repeatable, and consistently applied.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">In addition to this, with the <strong>Azure Backup MCP Server</strong>, these capabilities become programmable. Organizations can integrate backup posture validation, recovery readiness checks, and policy-driven restore workflows into automated systems while maintaining full control within sovereignty boundaries.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="how-you-can-build-resilience-in-azure">How you can build Resilience in Azure</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">On Azure, this evolution reflects a shift from predefined constructs to intentional architectures, from fragmented tools to unified experiences, and from guidance to execution. As organizations navigate increasing complexity, regulatory constraints, and unpredictable failure modes, the path forward is clear: build resilience into the foundation, validate it continuously, and automate it wherever possible. With Azure’s platform capabilities, application-centric experiences, and intelligent agents, resiliency is not just achievable but operationalized to deliver with confidence.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Explore <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/solutions/azure-essentials" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Azure Essentials</a> to get started with a unified resiliency experience across your applications and infrastructure. Azure Essentials, Microsoft Unified, and <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/solutions/azure-accelerate" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Azure Accelerate</a> help organizations move from resiliency design to operational execution across every stage of the lifecycle.</p>

		<title>External key management for Azure Managed HSM is now in public preview</title>
		<link>https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/blog/external-key-management-for-azure-managed-hsm-is-now-in-public-preview/</link>
					<comments>https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/blog/external-key-management-for-azure-managed-hsm-is-now-in-public-preview/#respond</comments>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Salim Chawro]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Tue, 07 Jul 2026 16:00:00 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Management and governance]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Azure]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Azure Key Vault API]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Azure Key Vault Managed HSM]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Azure RBAC]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Managed HSM]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/blog/?p=52575</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[<p>Azure Key Vault Managed Hardware Security Module (HSM) provides strong sovereignty over your encryption keys. Keys are generated and stored in a single-tenant, FIPS 140-3 Level 3 HSM that only you control: Microsoft has no access to your key material, and you govern who can use each key.</p>
<p>The post <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/blog/external-key-management-for-azure-managed-hsm-is-now-in-public-preview/">External key management for Azure Managed HSM is now in public preview</a> appeared first on <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/blog">Microsoft Azure Blog</a>.</p>
]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[
<p class="wp-block-paragraph"><a href="https://aka.ms/mhsmekm" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Azure Key Vault Managed Hardware Security Module (HSM)</a> provides strong sovereignty over your encryption keys. Keys are generated and stored in a single-tenant, FIPS 140-3 Level 3 HSM that only you control: Microsoft has no access to your key material, and you govern who can use each key. For most organizations, including those with stringent regulatory requirements, this level of control is sufficient.</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">
  Some organizations have a further requirement: the hardware that holds their key must reside physically outside Azure datacenters. <strong>External key management for Azure Key Vault Managed HSM is now in public preview</strong> to address that requirement, delivering on <a href="https://blogs.microsoft.com/blog/2025/06/16/announcing-comprehensive-sovereign-solutions-empowering-european-organizations/">a commitment made a year ago</a>.
</p>



<div class="wp-block-buttons is-content-justification-center is-layout-flex wp-container-core-buttons-is-layout-a89b3969 wp-block-buttons-is-layout-flex">
<div class="wp-block-button"><a class="wp-block-button__link wp-element-button" href="https://aka.ms/mhsmekm" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer noreferrer noopener"><strong style="font-family: inherit;font-size: 15.008px;font-style: inherit;letter-spacing: 0.30016px;text-transform: inherit">Try <strong>External key management for Azure Key Vault Managed HSM</strong></strong></a></div>
</div>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="how-managed-hsm-delivers-sovereignty-today"><a id="post-52575-X6153d177f958023adb85f2376ce55c910fb68bf"></a>How Managed HSM delivers sovereignty today</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">
  Before looking at external key management, it’s worth being precise about the sovereignty <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/key-vault/managed-hsm/overview">Managed HSM</a> already provides. Managed HSM is a single-tenant service: each instance is a dedicated cluster of FIPS 140-3 Level 3 validated HSM partitions for each customer—built on Marvell LiquidSecurity adapters. Keys are generated inside that hardware and never leave it in plaintext, making the keys inaccessible to Microsoft operators.
</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Control rests with you, not Microsoft:</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Customer-specific security domain.</strong> Each HSM cluster is cryptographically isolated by a security domain that you generate and own. Microsoft can’t decrypt your key material or recover your HSM cluster without it. You are in full control of the security domain as it’s protection and safeguarding is outside of Microsoft.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Multiperson control.</strong> The security domain is protected by a quorum of RSA key pairs that you hold offline. Recovery requires your quorum, so no single person—and no Microsoft operator—can act alone.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Local role-based access control</strong> (<strong>RBAC).</strong> A data-plane authorization model, independent of <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/role-based-access-control/overview">Azure RBAC</a>, governs who can perform each cryptographic operation.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Key attestation.</strong> You can obtain cryptographic proof that a key was generated and is used within the FIPS 140-3 Level 3 hardware boundary.</li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">
  Managed HSM is built on FIPS 140-3 Level 3 HSMs and confidential computing technology based on Intel SGX, so request handling, access control, and key material are isolated in hardware enclaves and HSMs that no Microsoft operator—even one with administrative or physical access to the host—can read. Managed HSM provides redundancy, isolation, and protection—giving organizations the sovereignty assurances they need without compromising on key security, operational overhead or availability. 
</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="what-external-key-management-adds"><a id="post-52575-what-external-key-management-adds"></a>What external key management adds</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">
  Managed HSM already provides full customer control over your keys, with enterprise-grade availability, security, and operational simplicity. External key management adds one capability: the option to keep your key material on an HSM that you own and operate, either on-premises or with a trusted third party, completely outside Microsoft infrastructure.
</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">
  External key management is designed for scenarios where regulation or contractual obligations mandate the cryptographic keys must reside outside the cloud provider’s environment. These requirements are sometimes found in highly regulated sectors such as government, financial services, and critical infrastructure, and in jurisdictions with strict data-sovereignty rules. External key management ensures the root of trust and key material remain on hardware you own and operate, outside Microsoft infrastructure, and under your direct physical control. 
</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">
  However, this model should only be adopted deliberately and only when required. For most workloads, Managed HSM keys remain the recommended approach, delivering higher native availability, reduced operational complexity, and a security posture that meets or exceeds sovereignty requirements without introducing additional risk or overhead. External key management is about meeting specific regulatory constraints, not increasing baseline security. When those constraints do not apply, Managed HSM provides a stronger, more reliable, and more operationally efficient solution.
</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="how-it-works"><a id="post-52575-how-it-works"></a>How it works</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">
  External key management extends Managed HSM through a dedicated API endpoint that connects directly to the HSM you control. It allows cryptographic operations in Azure to invoke external key material without changing how applications interact with the service. The external key never resides in or passes through Microsoft infrastructure; only your hardware uses it. Because you control that hardware, you can disconnect it at any time to halt all cryptographic operations.
</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Integration is transparent to applications.</strong> Applications continue to use Managed HSM and the Azure Key Vault API with the customer-managed key envelope encryption pattern unchanged. When an data access requires your external key to decrypt local data encryption keys, Managed HSM forwards it to your hardware and returns the result.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>You choose the hardware and partner.</strong> Because the external key management API is an open specification, you decide how to implement it. Your hardware, your partner, or your implementation.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>All connections are mutually authenticated and encrypted.</strong> Traffic between Azure and your hardware is secured with mutual TLS, ensuring a secure and trusted connection between <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us">Azure</a> and your HSM.</li>
</ul>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="hsm-ecosystem"><a id="post-52575-hsm-ecosystem"></a>HSM ecosystem</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">
  A growing ecosystem of HSM vendors support integration with the Managed HSM external key management API, as many providers are actively enabling compatibility for their platforms.
</p>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Microsoft doesn’t build or operate the connecting integration proxy itself. Instead, you benefit from an open model: you can use a vendor provided implementation, reply on a partner to operate it, or build your own.</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="responsibilities-and-tradeoffs"><a id="post-52575-responsibilities-and-tradeoffs"></a>Responsibilities and tradeoffs</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">
  External key management deliberately shifts a portion of operational responsibility to you. This is the direct consequence of extending the trust boundary beyond Azure: you gain control over the root of trust, and with it, ownership of the systems that enforce it.
</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Availability of your hardware.</strong> The Managed HSM SLA applies up to the point Managed HSM calls your external HSM proxy. Availability of your proxy and HSM is your responsibility. Any disruption on your side directly impacts cryptographic operations and Azure service data accessibility.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Scope of operations.</strong> External key management focuses on the operations used to protect data at rest. It does not expose the full set of key operations available with Managed HSM keys, reflecting a deliberate trade-off between control and functionality.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Hardware operations.</strong> Provisioning, securing, scaling, monitoring, and recovery of your proxy and HSM become your responsibility, whether operated directly or through a partner.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Error transparency.</strong> Failures originating on your side of the connection are surfaced in Managed HSM logs, but remain your responsibility to diagnose and resolve.</li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">
  This is the core trade-off: more control means more responsibility.
</p>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="public-preview-scope"><a id="post-52575-public-preview-scope"></a>Public preview scope</h2>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Availability:</strong> all Azure public regions at preview launch.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Access:</strong> gated. Your Microsoft account team enables external key management on your Managed HSM—contact them to request it.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Use case:</strong> protecting data at rest for <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/security/fundamentals/encryption-customer-managed-keys-support">Azure services that support customer-managed keys with Managed HSM</a>.</li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item"><strong>Pricing:</strong> standard <a href="https://azure.microsoft.com/en-us/pricing/details/key-vault/" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Managed HSM pricing</a>, with no additional Microsoft surcharge. You cover the cost of your own hardware and any partner licensing.</li>
</ul>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="get-started"><a id="post-52575-get-started"></a>Get started</h2>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li class="wp-block-list-item">Start with: <a href="https://aka.ms/mhsmekmstart" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">What is Managed HSM external key management?</a></li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Review the <a href="https://aka.ms/mhsmekmsla" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">SLA and shared-responsibility model</a></li>



<li class="wp-block-list-item">Try the <a href="https://learn.microsoft.com/azure/key-vault/managed-hsm/external-key-management-quickstart-cli" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Azure CLI quickstart</a></li>
</ul>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">
  External key management is the latest step in giving customers granular control over how and where their keys are protected. During public preview, your feedback will directly shape the feature on its path to general availability — including the operational guidance, vendor integrations, and scenarios we prioritize next.
</p>

<div class="promotional promotional--has-media promotional--media-right">
	<div class="promotional__wrapper">
		<div class="promotional__content-wrapper">
			<div class="promotional__content">
				

<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="take-control-of-your-encryption-keys">Take control of your encryption keys</h2>



<p class="wp-block-paragraph">Explore external key management for Managed HSM and keep your key material outside Azure while maintaining secure, scalable operations.</p>
